Definition and Proof: Simple and Composite
Definition of simple and composite, and proof that composite ends in simple using number analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions of simple and composite essences and the logical proof that every composite necessarily ends in a simple.

Core Idea
Every composite must contain simple parts, just as every number contains units.

This passage establishes a foundational principle in metaphysical reasoning: the distinction between simple and composite essences. The author not only defines these terms but also provides a rigorous logical proof that composition cannot go on infinitely—it must terminate in the simple. By studying this, you will grasp a key building block for arguments about the nature of reality.

Guided Path
1
Read the definitions of simple and composite
Start by reading the first part of the Arabic passage where the author defines simple (بسيط) as not composed of several things, and composite (مركب) as composed of several gathered things. Note that simple can have potential parts but not actual parts.
The commentator clarifies that a line is simple even though it has potential parts.
2
Understand the proof by infinite regress
Read the argument that every composite requires parts each of which is a single reality (one in actuality). If not, you get an infinite regress of parts within parts, which is impossible. Therefore, the composite must end in simple things.
This is a direct proof by contradiction.
3
Analyze the number analogy
The author strengthens the proof with an analogy: every number, whether finite or infinite, contains units (the ones). Similarly, every composite must contain indivisible units (simples). Make sure you see how the analogy makes the argument more intuitive.
Units of number are indivisible in actuality and potentiality; other simples are only indivisible in actuality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الماهية إما بسيطة لا تلتئم من عدة أمور تجتمع، أو مركبة تقابلها فهي التي تلتئم من عدة أمور مجتمعة ( وينتهي المركب إلى البسيط) إذ لا بد أن يكون في المركب أمور كل واحد منها حقيقة واحدة، وإلا لكان مركباً من أمور لا نهاية لها لا مرة واحدة بل مراراً غير متناهية ومع ذلك فلا بد من وجود البسيط
قوله : (إما بسيطة) قدمها مع أن مفهومها عدمي لتعلق حكم المركبة به . قوله : (تجتمع) ذكره لإفادة أن المعتبر في البسيط أن لا يكون أجزاء لها بالفعل، ولا يعتبر انتفاء الأجزاء بالقوة، فإن الخط والسطح والجسم التعليمي بسائط مع أن لها جزءاً بالقوة . قوله : (إذ لا بد أن يكون في المركب (أمور أي أمر أن كل واحد منهما متصف بالوحدة بالفعل بلا واسطة أو بواسطة أو بوسائط . قوله : ( وإلا لكان إلخ) أي وإن لم يكن كل واحد من تلك الأمور واحداً بالفعل كان بعضها مركباً من أمور غير متناهية بالفعل. قوله : (بل مراراً غير متناهية لأنه إذا فرض جزء منها بحيث لا ينتهي إلى البسيط كان ذلك الجزء مركباً من أمور غير متناهية وكذلك جزء الجزء وهو ما يبقى بعد إسقاط واحد من تلك
فيه) لأن العدد أي المتعدد بالفعل ( ولو) كان ( غير متناه فيه الواحد ) الذي لا تعدد فيه بالفعل ( ضرورة ) لأن الواحد مبدأ المتعدد، كما أن الوحدة مبدأ للعدد أن كما امتنع عدد متناه، أو غير متناه من غير أن يوجد فيه وحدات كذلك، يمتنع يوجد متعدد لا يكون فيه آحاد أي أمور غير منقسمة بالفعل، سواء كانت قابلة للانقسام أو لا .
قوله: (أي المتعدد (بالفعل) فسر العدد بذلك ليشمل الدليل كل مركب بالفعل فينطبق الدليل بالمدعى. قوله : ( كذلك يمتنع إلخ) لكن آحاد العدد وحدات حقيقة لا يمكن انقسامها بالفعل ولا بالقوة، بخلاف آحاد ما سواه فإنها لا بد أن تكون واحدة بالفعل ليتقوم بها المتعدد أعم من تكون واحدة بالقوة أيضاً أولا .
Essence is either simple, not composed of several things that come together, or composite, its opposite, which is composed of several things gathered together. (And the composite ends in the simple) for there must necessarily be in the composite things each of which is a single reality, otherwise it would be composed of things without end, not once but infinitely many times, and despite that the simple must exist.
His saying: (either simple) he presented it even though its concept is privative because the ruling of the composite is attached to it. His saying: (come together) he mentioned it to indicate that what is considered in the simple is that it does not have parts in actuality, and the absence of parts in potentiality is not considered; for the line, surface, and mathematical body are simple even though they have a part in potentiality. His saying: (for there must necessarily be in the composite) things i.e., a thing such that each one of them is characterized by oneness in actuality without intermediary or through an intermediary or through intermediaries. His saying: (otherwise it would be etc.) i.e., if each of those things were not one in actuality, then some of them would be composed of infinite things in actuality. His saying: (rather, infinitely many times) because if a part of it is assumed such that it does not end in the simple, then that part is composed of infinite things, and likewise the part of the part, which is what remains after dropping one from those.
(In it) because number, i.e., the multiple in actuality, even if it is infinite, contains the one which has no multiplicity in actuality of necessity, because the one is the principle of the multiple, just as oneness is the principle of number. For just as it is impossible for a finite or infinite number to exist without units in it, so it is impossible for a multiple to exist without having units, i.e., things not divided in actuality, whether they are divisible or not.
His saying: (i.e., the multiple in actuality) he interpreted number by that so that the proof includes every composite in actuality, so the proof applies to the claim. His saying: (thus it is impossible etc.) but the units of number are real units that cannot be divided in actuality or in potentiality, unlike the units of other things, for they must necessarily be one in actuality to constitute the multiple, whether they are also one in potentiality or not.
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage defines and proves a fundamental principle. The author first defines simple essence as not composed of several things, and composite essence as composed of several gathered things. He then states that every composite must end in the simple, because in the composite there must be things each of which is a single reality; otherwise an infinite regress would occur. The commentator explains that the simple is defined by not having actual parts, though it may have potential parts (like a line). The argument is then strengthened using the analogy of number: every number, even infinite, contains units (the one). Similarly, every multiple (composite) must contain indivisible units (the simple). The commentator clarifies that the units of number are indivisible in both actuality and potentiality, whereas the units of other composites need only be indivisible in actuality to constitute the multiple.

Key Terms
بسيط
مركب
العدد
الواحد
آحاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Infinite regress is possible; composition can go on forever without simple parts.
Correction: The passage proves that infinite regress is impossible because each composite would require infinite parts, leading to contradiction. Number analogy shows that just as number requires units, composite requires simples.

Checkpoint
What is the basis for the proof that composite must end in simple?
A) The empirical observation of physical objects
B) The principle that the one is the origin of number and every number contains units
C) The authority of ancient philosophers
D) The impossibility of defining simple things

Key Takeaway
The existence of composition logically entails the existence of simple, indivisible units, just as number entails units.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that a belief or practice you hold is complex and cannot be simplified? This lesson shows that every composite must have simple units. What is one area of your faith or life that you have not broken down to its core intention? Will you today seek to identify that simple unit (e.g., sincerity) that should underlie your multiple actions?
?
The proof uses the analogy of number: every multiple must contain the one. In your daily interactions, you encounter countless situations, decisions, and people. Do you see the One (God) as the principle behind all this multiplicity? This lesson demands you recognize that without that simple foundation, everything becomes an infinite regress of meaninglessness. How will you intentionally remind yourself today that every moment is a unit sustained by the One?
?
If the composite cannot exist without the simple, then your own character is built on fundamental traits. What is one composite habit (e.g., anger, procrastination, attachment to worldly things) that you have not traced back to its simple root (e.g., pride, fear, love for the world)? This lesson calls you to dissect that composite and repent from the simple sin at its core. Will you do that tonight?

The Four Types of Simple and Composite
The author enumerates the four types of simple and composite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four types of simple and composite based on reason and external existence.

Core Idea
Simple and composite are defined by their relation to reason or external existence.

Before discussing the proof, the author establishes a crucial classification: things can be simple or composite in two different domains—the intellect and the outside world. This yields four distinct categories. Understanding these categories is essential for the subsequent argument about the rational composite.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation once to get the overall classification.
2
Identify the four types
Notice each type: rational simple, external simple, rational composite, external composite. Look at how each is defined.
3
Understand the examples
What are the examples for each type? For instance, 'house' is an external composite.
The author mentions some examples directly, others implicitly.
4
Reflect on the distinction
Why is it important to separate rational from external simplicity? Think about how something can be simple in one domain but composite in another.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( وكلاهما يعتبر) بالقياس إلى العقل تارة (و) بالقياس إلى الخارج أخرى، فالأقسام أربعة : بسيط عقلي لا يلتئم في العقل من أمور عدة تجتمع فيه كالأجناس العالية والفصول البسيطة، وبسيط خارجي لا يلتئم من أمور كذلك في الخارج، كالمفارقات من العقول والنفوس فإنها بسيطة في الخارج وإن كانت مركبة في العقل، ومركب عقلي يلتئم من أمور تتمايز في العقل فقط، ومركب خارجي يلتئم من أجزاء متمايزة في الخارج كالبيت . ( والمركب العقلي، لو لم ينته إلى البسيط لزم محال آخر سوى ما ذكر ( وهو تعقل ما لا يتناهى وإنه محال ) إذا كان في زمان متناه ) فلا تكون الماهية المعقولة معقولة، وهذا إنما يتم في الماهيات المعقولة بالكنه. الأمور الغير المتناهية، وجزء جزء الجزء، وهلم جراً . فاندفع ما قيل: إنه إنما يلزم ذلك لو كان كل واحد من الأجزاء مركباً من أمور غير متناهية، أما إذا فرضنا أحد الأجزاء مركباً من أمور غير متناهية كما هو اللازم من رفع الإيجاب الكلي فلا .
قوله : (وكلاهما) كلمة كلا موضوعة للدلالة على الاثنين فمؤدى كلاهما وكل منهما واحد نحو جاءني الرجلان كلاهما . قوله : ( كالأجناس العالية على تقدير امتناع تركب الماهية من أمرين متساويين . قوله : ( مركبة في العقل على تقدير كون الجوهر جنساً. قوله : ( ومركب عقلي ) مثاله المفارقات، ولذا لم يذكر له مثالاً . قوله : ( متمايزة في الخارج لم يقل هاهنا فقط، لأن كل مركب في الخارج مركب في العقل. قوله : ( إذا كان إلخ) دفع بهذا التقييد استدراك قوله : وأنه محال بعد قوله : لزم محال . قوله : ( إنما يتم في الماهيات المعقولة بالكنه أي تفصيلاً، وكذا إنما يتم إذا كان تعقل الشيء بالكنه موقوفاً على تعقل ذاتياته بالكنه تفصيلاً، وكلا الأمرين في حيز المنع . قوله : ( كالأجناس العالية ) إذ لم يجوز التركيب من أمرين متساويين. قوله : ( تتمايز في العقل فقط ) لم يذكر له مثالاً لأن مثال البسيط الخارجي الذي ذكره مثال له .
(And both are considered) in relation to reason sometimes and in relation to the external another time, so the divisions are four: a rational simple that is not composed in the intellect from several things that come together in it, like the highest genera and the simple differentiate; an external simple that is not composed from such things in the external, like the separate entities of intellects and souls, for they are simple externally even though they are composite in the intellect; a rational composite that is composed from things that are distinguished only in the intellect; an external composite that is composed from parts distinguished externally like a house. (And the rational composite, if it does not end in the simple, another impossibility would follow besides what was mentioned, namely the comprehension of the infinite, and it is impossible if it is in a finite time, so the essence understood would not be understood; and this only applies to essences understood in essence. The infinite things, and part of part of part, and so on. So what was said is refuted: that this only follows if each one of the parts is composed of infinite things, but if we assume one of the parts is composed of infinite things as necessitated by the negation of the universal affirmative, then not.
His saying: (And both) the word 'kila' is placed to indicate duality, so the meaning of 'kilahuma' and each of them is one, like 'the two men came, both of them'. His saying: (like the highest genera) on the assumption that the composition of essence from two equal things is impossible. His saying: (composite in the intellect) on the assumption that substance is a genus. His saying: (and rational composite) its example is the separate entities, and thus he did not mention an example for it. His saying: (distinguished externally) he did not say 'only' here, because every composite externally is composite in the intellect. His saying: (if it is etc.) by this qualification he avoids the objection of his saying 'and it is impossible' after his saying 'an impossibility would follow'. His saying: (only applies to essences understood in essence) i.e., in detail, and similarly it only applies if the comprehension of a thing in essence depends on comprehending its essential properties in essence in detail, and both matters are subject to denial. His saying: (like the highest genera) because composition from two equal things is not allowed. His saying: (distinguished only in the intellect) he did not mention an example for it because the example of the external simple which he mentioned is an example for it.
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage classifies simple and composite into four types and adds a further proof. The author explains that both simple and composite can be considered in relation to the intellect or to external existence, yielding four categories: (1) rational simple, like highest genera and differentiate; (2) external simple, like immaterial intellects and souls; (3) rational composite, composed from parts distinct only in the intellect; (4) external composite, like a house. The author then presents an additional argument: if the rational composite does not end in the simple, then comprehending it would require grasping an infinite number of things in finite time, which is impossible. This argument only applies to essences known in their entirety. The commentator notes that the last two genera are not allowed to be composed of two equal things, and that the example of the external simple also serves for the rational simple.

Key Terms
بسيط عقلي
بسيط خارجي
مركب عقلي
مركب خارجي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that simple means undivided in all respects.
Correction: The passage shows simplicity is relative to a domain: something can be simple in reason but composite externally, or vice versa.

Checkpoint
Which of the four types is a house an example of?
A) Rational simple
B) External simple
C) Rational composite
D) External composite

Key Takeaway
The classification into four types is based on whether simplicity or compositeness is considered in the intellect or in external existence.

Proof: Rational Composite Must End in Simple
The author presents a proof for the necessity of simple in rational composites and refutes an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why if a rational composite does not end in simple components, it would lead to the impossible task of comprehending an infinite number of parts in finite time.

Core Idea
Infinite regress in rational composition makes understanding impossible.

The author now provides a proof that the rational composite must ultimately end in simple elements. Otherwise, to fully understand the composite, one would need to grasp an infinite chain of parts in a finite time, which is impossible. This argument also addresses an objection about the scope of the proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the conditional structure: 'if the rational composite does not end in simple...'
2
Identify the impossibility
What is the impossibility mentioned? The author says it leads to 'comprehension of the infinite' in finite time, which is impossible.
3
Understand the scope
Why does this only apply to essences known in their entirety (in detail)? Consider that superficial understanding may not require infinite parts.
4
See the refutation
The author refutes an objection: one might say the infinite regress only occurs if every part is infinitely complex. The author argues that the universal affirmative (every composite resolves to simples) forces the regress.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

( والمركب العقلي، لو لم ينته إلى البسيط لزم محال آخر سوى ما ذكر ( وهو تعقل ما لا يتناهى وإنه محال ) إذا كان في زمان متناه ) فلا تكون الماهية المعقولة معقولة، وهذا إنما يتم في الماهيات المعقولة بالكنه. الأمور الغير المتناهية، وجزء جزء الجزء، وهلم جراً . فاندفع ما قيل: إنه إنما يلزم ذلك لو كان كل واحد من الأجزاء مركباً من أمور غير متناهية، أما إذا فرضنا أحد الأجزاء مركباً من أمور غير متناهية كما هو اللازم من رفع الإيجاب الكلي فلا .

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that any composite must be understood part by part.
Correction: The proof shows that if parts are themselves composites ad infinitum, understanding becomes impossible, so composites must reduce to simples.

Checkpoint
What impossibility follows if a rational composite does not end in simple?
A) Infinite regress in definition
B) Comprehension of the infinite in finite time
C) Contradiction in terms
D) External existence becomes simple

Key Takeaway
Rational composites cannot be understood unless they ultimately break down into simple, unanalyzable components, otherwise an infinite regress makes understanding impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you try to understand a deep matter of faith, do you ever stop at vague slogans without breaking it down into its simple, essential truths? What would it cost you to truly grasp it in detail, and are you willing to pay that price?
?
Consider a doubt or confusion you have about your beliefs. If you trace it back step by step, does your chain of reasoning have an end in a simple certainty, or does it just keep going? How will you face the possibility that your lack of resolution might come from an infinite regress you've refused to stop?
?
The author says that understanding an infinite chain in finite time is impossible. Yet you live with a mind constantly flooded with questions. Are you content with superficial answers, or will you press until you hit the simple bedrock of truth — even if it means admitting you've built your understanding on shifting sand? What step will you take today to break one composite doubt down to its simplest element?

Logic and Technical Terms
intermediate
8 min
Simple and Composite Essences: Proof and Classification
Ijī's proof of simple parts and fourfold classification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why every composite must contain actual simple parts and how the four types of simple and composite are defined in relation to mind and external existence.

Core Idea
Every composite needs actual simples, just as number needs units.

This lesson begins with Ijī's proof that any composite, whether mental or physical, must have parts that are actually undivided. He draws an analogy from number: just as a number cannot exist without individual units, a composite cannot exist without simple components. He then classifies simple and composite into four kinds: mental simple, external simple, mental composite, and external composite. This classification is fundamental in Islamic metaphysics for analyzing essences.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof by analogy
Examine how Ijī uses the example of number to argue that every composite requires units (āḥād) that are actually indivisible. Note that these units may be potentially divisible.
The proof is a priori: it shows that multiplicity presupposes unity.
2
Identify the four categories
Look at the four definitions: mental simple, external simple, mental composite, external composite. Understand the difference between composition in the mind and in external reality.
Examples: highest genera are mental simples; separate intellects are external simples but mentally composite.
3
Apply the distinction
Consider how this classification helps in theological and philosophical discussions about the simplicity of God and created beings.
This will be important later when discussing attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثاني - المقصد الرابع: الماهية إما بسيطة أو مركبة فيه ) لأن العدد أي المتعدد بالفعل ( ولو) كان ( غير متناه فيه الواحد ) الذي لا تعدد فيه بالفعل ( ضرورة ) لأن الواحد مبدأ للعدد أن فكما امتنع عدد متناه، أو غير متناه من غير أن يوجد فيه وحدات كذلك، يمتنع يوجد متعدد لا يكون فيه آحاد أي أمور غير منقسمة بالفعل، سواء كانت قابلة للانقسام أو لا . ( وكلاهما يعتبر) بالقياس إلى العقل تارة (و) بالقياس إلى الخارج أخرى، فالأقسام أربعة : بسيط عقلي لا يلتئم في العقل من أمور عدة تجتمع فيه كالأجناس العالية والفصول البسيطة، وبسيط خارجي لا يلتئم من أمور كذلك في الخارج، كالمفارقات من العقول والنفوس فإنها بسيطة في الخارج وإن كانت مركبة في العقل، ومركب عقلي يلتئم من أمور تتمايز في العقل فقط، ومركب خارجي يلتئم من أجزاء متمايزة في الخارج كالبيت . ( والمركب العقلي، لو لم ينته إلى البسيط لزم محال آخر سوى ما ذكر ( وهو تعقل ما لا يتناهى وإنه محال ) إذا كان في زمان متناه ) فلا تكون الماهية المعقولة معقولة، وهذا إنما يتم في الماهيات المعقولة بالكنه. الأمور الغير المتناهية، وجزء جزء الجزء، وهلم جراً . فاندفع ما قيل: إنه إنما يلزم ذلك لو كان كل واحد من الأجزاء مركباً من أمور غير متناهية، أما إذا فرضنا أحد الأجزاء مركباً من أمور غير متناهية كما هو اللازم من رفع الإيجاب الكلي فلا . قوله : (أي المتعدد (بالفعل فسر العدد بذلك ليشمل الدليل كل مركب بالفعل فينطبق الدليل بالمدعى. قوله : ( كذلك يمتنع إلخ) لكن آحاد العدد وحدات حقيقة لا يمكن انقسامها بالفعل ولا بالقوة، بخلاف آحاد ما سواه فإنها لا بد أن تكون واحدة بالفعل ليتقوم بها المتعدد أعم من تكون واحدة بالقوة أيضاً أولا . أن قوله : ( وكلاهما) كلمة كلا موضوعة للدلالة على الاثنين فمؤدى كلاهما وكل منهما واحد نحو جاءني الرجلان كلاهما . قوله : ( كالأجناس العالية على تقدير امتناع تركب الماهية من أمرين متساويين . قوله : ( مركبة في العقل على تقدير كون الجوهر جنساً. قوله : ( ومركب عقلي ) مثاله المفارقات، ولذا لم يذكر له مثالاً . قوله : ( متمايزة في الخارج لم يقل هاهنا فقط، لأن كل مركب في الخارج مركب في العقل. قوله : ( إذا كان إلخ) دفع بهذا التقييد استدراك قوله : وأنه محال بعد قوله : لزم محال . قوله : ( إنما يتم في الماهيات المعقولة بالكنه أي تفصيلاً، وكذا إنما يتم إذا كان تعقل الشيء بالكنه موقوفاً على تعقل ذاتياته بالكنه تفصيلاً، وكلا الأمرين في حيز المنع . قوله : ( كالأجناس العالية ) إذ لم يجوز التركيب من أمرين متساويين. قوله : ( تتمايز في العقل فقط ) لم يذكر له مثالاً لأن مثال البسيط الخارجي الذي ذكره مثال له .
The second observation - the fourth aim: The essence is either simple or composite. In it, because number, i.e., the actually multiple, even if it were infinite, requires the one, which has no actual multiplicity, necessarily, because the one is the principle of number. So just as a finite or infinite number without containing units is impossible, likewise it is impossible for there to exist a multiple without containing units, i.e., things actually indivisible, whether they are divisible potentially or not. And both are considered in relation to the mind sometimes and in relation to external existence other times. Thus the divisions are four: mental simple, which is not composed in the mind from several things that combine in it, like the highest genera and simple differentiae; external simple, which is not composed from such things in external existence, like the separate intellects and souls, for they are simple externally though composite in the mind; mental composite, which is composed from things that are distinguished only in the mind; and external composite, which is composed from parts distinguished externally, like a house. And as for the mental composite, if it does not terminate in the simple, another impossibility results besides what was mentioned, namely the intellection of the infinite, and it is impossible if it is in finite time. Then the intelligible essence would not be intelligible. And this is only complete in essences known by essence. The infinite things, and part of part of part, and so on. Thus what was said is refuted: that this only follows if each one of the parts is composed of infinite things; but if we suppose one of the parts composed of infinite things, as is necessary from lifting the universal affirmation, then it does not follow. He said: (i.e., the actually multiple) he interpreted number by that to include the proof for every actually composite, so the proof applies to the claim. He said: (likewise it is impossible, etc.) but the units of number are true units that cannot be divided actually nor potentially, unlike the units of other things, for they must be actually one to constitute the multiple, whether they are also potentially one or not. He said: (and both) the word 'kila' is used to indicate two, so the meaning of 'kilāhuma' and 'kullun minhumā' is one, like 'jā'anī al-rajulāni kilāhumā'. He said: (like the highest genera) on the assumption of the impossibility of the composition of the essence from two co-equal things. He said: (composite in the mind) on the assumption that substance is a genus. He said: (and mental composite) its example is the separate entities, so he did not give an example. He said: (distinguished externally) he did not say 'only' here, because every external composite is also mentally composite. He said: (if it is in finite time) by this restriction he repelled the objection to his statement: and it is impossible, after his statement: another impossibility results. He said: (this is only complete in essences known by essence) i.e., in detail, and likewise it is only complete if intellection of a thing by essence is dependent on intellection of its essentials by essence in detail, and both matters are subject to denial. He said: (like the highest genera) since composition from two co-equal things is not allowed. He said: (distinguished only in the mind) he did not give an example because the example of the external simple he gave suffices for it.
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage combines a proof and a classification. It begins by stating the topic: essence (māhiyya) is either simple or composite. The proof that every composite must have actual simple units uses number as an analogy: just as any number, finite or infinite, must have individual units that are actually indivisible, so any multiple (composite) must have actual simples (āḥād) that are actually undivided, whether they are potentially divisible or not. This establishes the necessity of simple parts. The classification follows: simple and composite are each considered in two ways—in the mind (aqlī) and in external existence (khārijī), yielding four types. The author defines each: mental simple (e.g., highest genera and simple differentiae), external simple (e.g., separate intellects and souls, which are simple externally but composite mentally), mental composite (composed only mentally), and external composite (composed of parts distinct externally, e.g., a house). The passage then presents a second proof: a mental composite must terminate in a simple, otherwise it would involve intellection of an infinite regress in finite time, which is impossible. It clarifies that this argument only applies to essences known in full (bi-al-kunnih). An objection is considered and refuted: if one part were composed of infinite things, the regress need not be in every part. The remainder of the passage contains commentary (sharh) explaining specific phrases from the main text, such as interpreting 'number' as actually multiple, the meaning of 'units', grammatical remarks on 'kilāhumā', examples for the kinds, and the defense of the proof's conditions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Simple parts are indivisible in every sense.
Correction: Ijī states they are actually indivisible but may be divisible potentially.

Checkpoint
What is the main analogy used to prove that every composite requires simple parts?
A) One is the principle of number, so composite requires units.
B) Simple is prior to composite.
C) External composites require mental simples.
D) The author classifies them that way.

Key Takeaway
Every composite, whether mental or external, must contain parts that are actually undivided, and these parts can be categorized by their mode of composition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Reflect on your own identity. Is your sense of self simple or composite? According to the classification in this lesson, how would you categorize it? What evidence from your experience supports that categorization?
?
Consider a belief you hold that is complex—like your faith. What are the simple, indivisible components of that belief? Are you certain they are indeed indivisible? Why?
?
In what ways do you simplify complex realities in your own mind? Could you be reducing things to mental simples that are not actually simple? How does this lesson challenge your intellectual humility and honesty?

Logic and Technical Terms

6 min
The Termination of Mental Composites in Simples
Ijī's second proof that mental composites terminate in simples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that a mental composite must eventually lead to simple components, otherwise an infinite regress would make intellection impossible.

Core Idea
Mental composites without simple foundations lead to impossible infinite regress.

Ijī presents a second argument concerning mental composites. If a composite in the mind did not eventually consist of simple parts, understanding it would require grasping an infinite chain of parts in a finite time, which is impossible. He qualifies that this applies only to essences known in full detail (bi-al-kunnih). An objection is raised and refuted.

Guided Path
1
Understand the infinite regress argument
See how Ijī argues that if a mental composite doesn't terminate in simples, then to know it you'd need to know an infinite number of parts, which is impossible in finite time.
This is a distinct proof from the earlier number analogy.
2
Note the qualification
The argument only applies to essences known in their full essence (bi-al-kunnih), i.e., detailed knowledge of all essential parts.
3
Examine the objection and response
An objector says the regress could stop if one part itself has infinite parts. Ijī refutes this by showing that the universal claim still requires regress.
The refutation relies on the necessity of the universal affirmation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The second observation - the fourth aim: The essence is either simple or composite. In it, because number, i.e., the actually multiple, even if it were infinite, requires the one, which has no actual multiplicity, necessarily, because the one is the principle of number. So just as a finite or infinite number without containing units is impossible, likewise it is impossible for there to exist a multiple without containing units, i.e., things actually indivisible, whether they are divisible potentially or not. Commentary
This passage combines a proof and a classification. It begins by stating the topic: essence (māhiyya) is either simple or composite. The proof that every composite must have actual simple units uses number as an analogy: just as any number, finite or infinite, must have individual units that are actually indivisible, so any multiple (composite) must have actual simples (āḥād) that are actually undivided, whether they are potentially divisible or not. The classification follows: simple and composite are each considered in two ways—in the mind (aqlī) and in external existence (khārijī), yielding four types. The author defines each: mental simple (e.g., highest genera and simple differentiae), external simple (e.g., separate intellects and souls, which are simple externally but composite mentally), mental composite (composed only mentally), and external composite (composed of parts distinct externally, e.g., a house). The passage then presents a second proof: a mental composite must terminate in a simple, otherwise it would involve intellection of an infinite regress in finite time, which is impossible. It clarifies that this argument only applies to essences known in full (bi-al-kunnih). An objection is considered and refuted: if one part were composed of infinite things, the regress need not be in every part. Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The infinite regress argument applies to all knowledge of composites.
Correction: It only applies to detailed knowledge of essences (bi-al-kunnih), not to all knowledge.

Checkpoint
What is the key qualification of the second proof?
A) It applies only to mental composites.
B) It applies only to essences known by essence (bi-al-kunnih).
C) It applies only to external simples.
D) It applies only to infinite numbers.

Key Takeaway
Mental composites must terminate in simple parts; otherwise, detailed knowledge of them would require an impossible infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a deep conviction you hold about your faith. Can you trace it back to a simple, irreducible belief that you accept without proof? Are you willing to rest your certainty on that simple foundation, even if you cannot dissect it further?
?
This lesson argues that without simple parts, knowledge would be impossible in finite time. In your daily rush to 'understand everything,' do you ever acknowledge that your mind has limits? How does accepting your finitude change the way you approach the infinite nature of Allah?
?
The author refutes an objection by insisting that even one infinite part makes the whole impossible to fully know. Is there an area of your life where you cling to complexity to avoid a simple, demanding truth? What simple step is your heart resisting?

Division of Parts: Overlapping and Disparate
Classification of parts of composite essences into overlapping, disparate, and the special case of equal parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the classification of parts of a composite essence into overlapping and disparate, and the debate about where equal parts belong.

Core Idea
The parts of a composite essence are either overlapping or disparate, with equal parts posing a puzzle.

This passage from a kalam textbook classifies the parts that make up a composite essence. The author presents a basic division into overlapping and disparate, but the well-known view adds nuance. As you read, notice how the commentator refines the classification and addresses the tricky case of equal parts.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage slowly, then the literal translation. Note the main classification: overlapping (mutadākhila) vs. disparate (mutabāyina).
The passage includes both matn and sharh; focus on the matn's structure.
2
Identify the three categories
The passage mentions overlapping, disparate, and a potential third: equal (mutasāwiya). Observe how the commentator suggests dividing parts into co-extensive (mutasādiqa) and disparate, then subdividing co-extensive.
Equal parts are a subset of co-extensive but distinct from overlapping in the narrow sense.
3
Understand the logical criteria
Learn the criterion for equality: each part is predicated of every individual of the other. Contrast with overlapping (one more general) and disparate (no overlap).
The example 'sentient' and 'moving by will' illustrates equal parts when considered as components of 'animal'.

Source Text

المرصد الثاني - المقصد الخامس : في تقسيم الأجزاء [ المقصد الخامس : في تقسيم الأجزاء ] ( في تقسيم الأجزاء ) للماهية المركبة ( وهو من وجهين : الأول أنها إن صدق بعضها على بعض فمتداخلة ) ، سواء كانت متساوية أو غير متساوية ( وإلا فمتباينة ) . والمشهور أن المتداخلة ما يكون بعضها أعم من بعض، فلا يتناول المتساوية، فيحتاج إلى جعلها قسماً ثالثاً، والأظهر في العبارة أن يقسم الأجزاء إلى متصادقة ومتباينة، ثم يقسم المتصادقة إلى متداخله ومتساوية . ( أما المتداخلة فإن صدق كل منهما على كل أفراد الآخر فهما متساويان نحو الحساس والمتحرك (بالإرادة إذا اعتبر ماهية مركبة قوله: (في تقسيم الأجزاء) أي أقل ما يحصل به التركيب وهو الجزآن، فإذا كانت زائدة يكون فيها اجتماع الأقسام المذكورة. قوله: (فمتداخلة) أي كلاً أو بعضاً . قوله: (فمتباينة) أي كلاً. قوله : ( فيحتاج إلخ) أو يقال بامتناع تركب الماهية عن المتساوية، وفيه نظر. وأما إدراجها في المتباينة فبعيد . قوله : ( والأظهر في العبارة إلخ أما بالقياس إلى ما قاله المصنف فلعدم إطلاق المتداخلة على غير المتعارف وأما بالقياس إلى المشهور فلإيهامه الانقسام إلى الاقسام الثلاثة هذا وإنما قال : في العبارة؛ لاتحاد الكل في المآل وهو التقسيم إلى الأقسام الثلاثة. قوله : ( فإن صدق كل منهما إلخ) صدق الكلي على أفراده وكذا الحال في التباين والعموم مطلقاً أو من وجه فالإنسان والكلي متباينان إن اختص أفراد الإنسان بأشخاصه؛ إذ لا شيء من الإنسان بكلي وهو ظاهر، ولا شيء من الكلي بإنسان؛ إذ لا يصدق الإنسان على شيء من أفراد الكلي صدق الكلي على الأفراد بل متحد به وإن جعل أفراده شاملة للأصناف أيضاً كان بينهما عموم من وجه وهو ظاهر قوله: (فإن صدق كل منهما على كل أفراد الآخر فهما متساويان) كما أن المعتبر في المساواة صدق كل منهما على كل أفراد الآخر، كذلك المعتبر في العموم مطلقاً صدق أحدهما على كل أفراد الآخر دون العكس، فليس مفهوم الكل أعم مطلقاً من مفهوم الإنسان لصدقه بدونه في الأشخاص، بل إما عام منه من وجه إذا اعتبر تصادقهما في الأصناف الإنسانية أو مباين له إن ادعى انحصار ما صدق عليه الإنسان في الأشخاص ولا يقدح في التباين حمل الكلي على الإنسان لأنه إنما يحمل على مفهومه كما في القضايا الطبيعية وذلك كحمل مفهوم الكلي الحقيقي على مفهوم الكلى الجزئي الحقيقي مع تباينهما اتفاقاً، والتباين إنما ينقدح إذا صدق أحدهما على ما صدق عليه الآخر. قوله : ( إذا اعتبر ماهية مركبة (منهما فإن قلت : الحيوان جسم نام حساس متحرك بالإرادة
The second observation - the fifth aim: On the division of parts. [The fifth aim: On the division of parts.] (On the division of parts) of the composite essence, and it is in two respects: first, that if some of them are predicated of some, then they are overlapping, whether equal or unequal; otherwise, disparate. The well-known view is that overlapping is when some of them are more general than others, so it does not include equals, thus they need to be made a third division. The most apparent in expression is to divide the parts into co-extensive (mutasādiqa) and disparate, then divide the co-extensive into overlapping and equal. (As for overlapping, if each of them is predicated of every individual of the other, then they are equal, like sentient and moving (by will) when considered as a composite essence. He said: (in the division of parts) i.e., the least by which composition occurs, namely two parts; if they are more, the mentioned divisions come together in them. He said: (so overlapping) i.e., wholly or partially. He said: (so disparate) i.e., wholly. He said: (so they need, etc.) Or it is said that composition of an essence from equals is impossible, but that is questionable. Including them in the disparate is far. He said: (and the most apparent in expression, etc.) In relation to what the author said, because 'overlapping' is not applied to the non-conventional, and in relation to the well-known, because it suggests division into three sections. This, and he said 'in expression' because everything is unified in outcome, which is the division into three sections. He said: (for if each of them is predicated, etc.) The predication of the universal over its individuals, and likewise in the case of disparity and absolute generality or generality from one aspect. For example, 'human' and 'universal' are disparate if the individuals of human are restricted to persons; since no human is a universal, and no universal is a human, because 'human' is not predicated of any individual of 'universal' as a universal over individuals—rather it is identical with it. If its individuals are made to include species as well, they have generality from one aspect, which is apparent. He said: (for if each of them is predicated of every individual of the other, then they are equal) Just as the criterion for equality is that each is predicated of every individual of the other, so the criterion for absolute generality is that one is predicated of every individual of the other without the converse. So the concept of 'whole' is not absolutely more general than the concept of 'human', because it is predicated of [things] without it in persons. Rather, either it is general from one aspect if their co-extensiveness in human species is considered, or disparate if the predication of 'human' is claimed to be restricted to persons. It does not harm disparity to apply 'universal' to 'human' because it is applied to its concept as in natural propositions, like applying the concept of real universal to the concept of real particular universal, despite their disparity by agreement. Disparity arises only when one is predicated of what the other is predicated of. He said: (when considered a composite essence) from them, for if you say: animal is a growing, sentient, moving (by will) body.
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage classifies the parts of a composite essence. The main text (matn) states that parts are divided in two respects: first, if some parts (here meaning the two parts of a composite) are predicated of each other (ṣadaqa ba‘ḍuhā ‘alā ba‘ḍ), they are overlapping (mutadākhila), whether they are equal or unequal; otherwise they are disparate (mutabāyina). The commentator clarifies that the well-known definition of overlapping is that one part is more general than the other, which excludes equal overlapping (e.g., 'sentient' and 'moving by will' if considered as parts of a composite like animal). This forces a third category. The preferred expression, according to the commentator, is to divide parts into co-extensive (mutasādiqa) and disparate, then subdivide co-extensive into overlapping (where one is more general) and equal. An example: if parts are such that each applies to all individuals of the other, they are equal—like 'sentient' and 'moving by will' when taken as components of 'animal'. The commentary then explains each phrase: 'overlapping' can be wholly or partially; 'disparate' is wholly; the issue of equals is discussed—some say composition from equals is impossible, but that is questionable. The commentator also elaborates on the logic of predication: absolute generality vs. equality and how to determine disparity. He gives the example of 'human' and 'universal' to illustrate disparity and co-extensiveness. The passage concludes mid-sentence with an example that begins 'animal is a growing, sentient, moving by will body.'

Key Terms
متداخلة
متباينة
متساوية
متصادقة
صدق
أعم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Equal parts are considered overlapping in the narrow sense (i.e., one more general).
Correction: Equal parts are not overlapping in the well-known definition; they are a separate category within co-extensive parts, as each is predicated of every individual of the other.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when are two parts considered equal?
A) When they overlap partially.
B) When each is predicated of every individual of the other.
C) When one is more general than the other.
D) When they are disparate.

Key Takeaway
The parts of a composite essence are primarily divided into co-extensive (which includes overlapping and equal) and disparate, based on logical predication.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Think of a theological idea that you have considered simple. After this lesson, can you identify its composite parts? What does that reveal about the depth of your understanding?
?

Overlapping and Distinct Parts
Al-Iji defines and refines the division of parts of a composite essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the classification of parts of a composite essence into overlapping (interpenetrating) and distinct, and why the standard definition is refined to include equal parts as a third category.

Core Idea
Parts of a composite essence are classified by how they relate to each other.

Before analyzing composite essences, we need a clear vocabulary for the relations between their parts. Al-Iji begins by dividing parts into two types based on whether they apply to each other. But this simple division leaves out equal parts, requiring a refinement. In this lesson, we'll explore the initial classification, the problem it creates, and the improved version that properly accounts for all relations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its literal translation. Get a feel for the technical terms and the flow of the argument.
Notice that the passage includes both the author's text and the commentator's gloss.
2
Identify the two types of parts
Find where Al-Iji states the two aspects of parts: interpenetrating and distinct. Understand the condition: if one part applies to the other, they are interpenetrating; otherwise distinct.
Interpenetrating includes cases where parts are equal or one is more general.
3
Understand the problem with the common definition
The commentary points out that the well-known definition restricts 'interpenetrating' to cases of generality, excluding equality. This forces a third division for equal parts. Reflect on why this is problematic.
The author's own definition already includes equals, but the common usage does not.
4
Learn the refined classification
The commentator proposes dividing parts first into co-extensive (mutually applying) and distinct, then subdividing co-extensive into interpenetrating (generality) and equal. See how this resolves the issue.
This ensures all three relations are covered: equality, generality-specification, and distinctness.
5
Review key terms and takeaway
Go through the key Arabic terms and their meanings. Then close with the key takeaway: the classification of parts is essential for analyzing composite essences in logic and metaphysics.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثاني - المقصد الخامس : في تقسيم الأجزاء
في تقسيم الأجزاء للماهية المركبة وهو من وجهين : الأول أنها إن صدق بعضها على بعض فمتداخلة سواء كانت متساوية أو غير متساوية وإلا فمتباينة . والمشهور أن المتداخلة ما يكون بعضها أعم من بعض، فلا يتناول المتساوية، فيحتاج إلى جعلها قسماً ثالثاً، والأظهر في العبارة أن يقسم الأجزاء إلى متصادقة ومتباينة، ثم يقسم المتصادقة إلى متداخله ومتساوية. قوله: (في تقسيم الأجزاء) أي أقل ما يحصل به التركيب وهو الجزآن، فإذا كانت زائدة يكون فيها اجتماع الأقسام المذكورة. قوله: (فمتداخلة) أي كلاً أو بعضاً . قوله: (فمتباينة) أي كلاً. قوله : ( فيحتاج إلخ) أو يقال بامتناع تركب الماهية عن المتساوية، وفيه نظر. وأما إدراجها في المتباينة فبعيد . قوله : ( والأظهر في العبارة إلخ أما بالقياس إلى ما قاله المصنف فلعدم إطلاق المتداخلة على غير المتعارف وأما بالقياس إلى المشهور فلإيهامه الانقسام إلى الاقسام الثلاثة هذا وإنما قال : في العبارة؛ لاتحاد الكل في المآل وهو التقسيم إلى الأقسام الثلاثة.
The second observation - the fifth objective: On the division of parts. On the division of parts of the composite essence. And it has two aspects: the first is that if one of them applies to the other, then they are interpenetrating, whether they are equal or not equal; otherwise they are distinct. And the well-known (view) is that interpenetrating is what has one part more general than another, so it does not include equals, so it needs to be made a third division. The most apparent in expression is that parts are divided into co-extensive and distinct, then the co-extensive is divided into interpenetrating and equal. His saying: (On the division of parts) i.e. the least that composition occurs with, which is the two parts, so if they are more, there occurs in them the combination of the mentioned divisions. His saying: (interpenetrating) i.e. wholly or partially. His saying: (distinct) i.e. wholly. His saying: (so it needs etc.) or it is said by the impossibility of composition of the essence from equals, and there is consideration in it. And as for including them in the distinct, it is far. His saying: (and the most apparent in expression etc.) as for in relation to what the author said, it is due to the non-application of 'interpenetrating' to the non-conventional, and as for in relation to the well-known, it is due to its suggesting division into three divisions. This, and he only said 'in expression' because the whole is one in outcome, which is division into three divisions.
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage defines the two types of parts of a composite essence: interpenetrating (متداخلة) and distinct (متباينة). The author first states the basic definition: if one part applies to the other (i.e., they share application in some way), they are interpenetrating; otherwise they are distinct. He notes that this includes equal and unequal cases. Then the commentator points out that the common definition restricts 'interpenetrating' to cases where one part is more general than the other, thereby excluding equal parts. This omission necessitates a third division (equal parts). To avoid this, a better classification is to first divide parts into co-extensive (متصادقة) and distinct, and then subdivide co-extensive into interpenetrating and equal. The commentator explains each phrase: 'on the division of parts' means the minimum composition requires two parts; 'interpenetrating' means mutual application wholly or partially; 'distinct' means no mutual application; and he discusses the objection about equal parts and the reason for the better wording. This ensures the classification covers all relations: equality, generality-specification, and distinctness.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'interpenetrating' only means one part is more general than the other.
Correction: Interpenetrating includes both equality and generality; equality is a specific subtype.

Checkpoint

A) Interpenetrating, distinct, and equal
B) Co-extensive, distinct, and interpenetrating
C) General, specific, and equal
D) Overlapping, separate, and identical

Key Takeaway
Parts of a composite essence are classified into co-extensive (interpenetrating and equal) and distinct, covering all logical relations.

Equal Overlapping Parts: Definition and Example
Al-Jurjani defines equal overlapping parts and provides an example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what it means for two universals to be equal in terms of overlapping parts, and how this differs from other relations like absolute generality and distinctness.

Core Idea
Equality means each universal applies to every individual of the other — co-extension, not identity.

Al-Jurjani now examines the specific case where overlapping universals have the same extension. This is the relation of equality (tasāwī). He gives a concrete example involving sensible and voluntary mover, and clarifies the logical conditions that define equality against other relations like generality from one aspect. Understanding this helps distinguish mere co-extension from conceptual identity.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of equality
Read the opening statement: if each universal applies to every individual of the other, they are equal. Note that this is mutual full application.
This is the core definition.
2
Analyze the example
Consider the example 'sensible' and 'voluntary mover' when a composite essence is considered. Why does this illustrate equality? Because every sensible being is a voluntary mover and vice versa, at least under that composite consideration.
The composite essence ensures the co-extension.
3
Compare with other relations
The commentary contrasts equality with absolute generality (one applies to all of the other but not vice versa) and distinctness (no overlap). Use the human/universal example to see these distinctions.
Equality is the strongest overlap.
4
Grasp the nuance about composite essence
The phrase 'when a composite essence is considered' is crucial. It indicates that the equality depends on how we define the universals. Without the composite, they might not be equal.
This prevents misunderstanding that equality is inherent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما المتداخلة فإن صدق كل منهما على كل أفراد الآخر فهما متساويان نحو الحساس والمتحرك بالإرادة إذا اعتبر ماهية مركبة منهما. قوله : ( فإن صدق كل منهما إلخ) صدق الكلي على أفراده وكذا الحال في التباين والعموم مطلقاً أو من وجه فالإنسان والكلي متباينان إن اختص أفراد الإنسان بأشخاصه؛ إذ لا شيء من الإنسان بكلي وهو ظاهر، ولا شيء من الكلي بإنسان؛ إذ لا يصدق الإنسان على شيء من أفراد الكلي صدق الكلي على الأفراد بل متحد به وإن جعل أفراده شاملة للأصناف أيضاً كان بينهما عموم من وجه وهو ظاهر. قوله: (فإن صدق كل منهما على كل أفراد الآخر فهما متساويان) كما أن المعتبر في المساواة صدق كل منهما على كل أفراد الآخر، كذلك المعتبر في العموم مطلقاً صدق أحدهما على كل أفراد الآخر دون العكس، فليس مفهوم الكل أعم مطلقاً من مفهوم الإنسان لصدقه بدونه في الأشخاص، بل إما عام منه من وجه إذا اعتبر تصادقهما في الأصناف الإنسانية أو مباين له إن ادعى انحصار ما صدق عليه الإنسان في الأشخاص ولا يقدح في التباين حمل الكلي على الإنسان لأنه إنما يحمل على مفهومه كما في القضايا الطبيعية وذلك كحمل مفهوم الكلي الحقيقي على مفهوم الكلى الجزئي الحقيقي مع تباينهما اتفاقاً، والتباين إنما ينقدح إذا صدق أحدهما على ما صدق عليه الآخر. قوله : ( إذا اعتبر ماهية مركبة (منهما فإن قلت : الحيوان جسم نام حساس متحرك بالإرادة
As for the interpenetrating, if each of the two applies to every individual of the other, then they are equal, like 'sensible' and 'voluntary mover' when a composite essence is considered from them. His saying: (if each of them applies etc.) The application of the universal to its individuals, and likewise the case in distinctness and generality absolutely or from one aspect. For 'human' and 'universal' are distinct if the individuals of human are restricted to its persons; since nothing of human is universal—this is apparent—and nothing of universal is human, since 'human' does not apply to any individual of the universal as the universal applies to individuals, rather it is identical with it. And if its individuals are made to include species also, there is between them generality from one aspect, which is apparent. His saying: (if each of them applies to every individual of the other, then they are equal) just as in equality the criterion is that each applies to every individual of the other, likewise in absolute generality the criterion is that one applies to every individual of the other without the converse. So the concept 'whole' is not absolutely more general than the concept 'human' because it applies without it in persons, rather it is either general from one aspect if their co-extension is considered in human species, or distinct from it if it is claimed that the application of 'human' is restricted to persons. And it does not prejudice distinctness that the universal is predicated of 'human' because it is only predicated of its concept as in natural propositions, like the predication of the concept of real universal on the concept of real particular universal while they are distinct by agreement, and distinctness only occurs if one applies to what the other applies to. His saying: (when a composite essence is considered from them, for if you say: animal is a growing, sensing, voluntary mover)
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage defines and explains the case of equal interpenetrating parts. When each part applies to every individual of the other, they are equal (متساويان). The example given is 'sensible' (حساس) and 'voluntary mover' (متحرك بالإرادة) when considered as a composite essence. The commentator elaborates on the meaning of application of universals to their individuals, distinguishing between equality, absolute generality, and generality from one aspect. He uses the example of 'human' and 'universal' to illustrate that if 'human' is restricted to persons, they are distinct, but if 'human' includes species, they have generality from one aspect. He also clarifies that the criterion for equality is mutual universal application, while for absolute generality it is one-sided universal application. The commentary ensures the reader understands the precise logical conditions for each relation.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think equality means identical concepts or definitions.
Correction: Equality refers only to co-extension (same individuals), not conceptual identity. The example of sensible and voluntary mover shows two distinct meanings that happen to apply to the same things under a composite essence.

?

?

?

Absolute Generality: Two Ways of Constitution
Al-Jurjani explains the two subcases of absolute generality: general constitutes specific or specific constitutes general.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two subcases of absolute generality: where the general constitutes the specific and where the specific constitutes the general, with examples from mental and real essences.

Core Idea
In absolute generality, constitution can go either way: general→specific or specific→general.

In logic, when two terms have a relation of absolute generality and specificity, we need to see which one constitutes the other. Al-Jurjani presents two possibilities: either the general constitutes the specific, as in 'white body', or the specific constitutes the general, as in 'rational animal'. This lesson unpacks these subcases with their examples and the logical principles behind them.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation to get the overall meaning.
This passage is dense but structured.
2
Identify the two subcases
Find where al-Jurjani presents the two possibilities: general constitutes specific, and specific constitutes general.
They are introduced by 'إما أن يقوم العام الخاص' and 'أو لا يقوم العام الخاص بل يكون الأمر بالعكس'.
3
Analyze the examples
Look at the examples 'white body' and 'rational animal'. In 'white body', the general 'body' constitutes the specific 'white'. In 'rational animal', the specific 'rational' constitutes the general 'animal'.
Notice that 'white body' is a mental composite, while 'rational animal' is a real composite.
4
Understand the logical principles
Understand why the general is self-constituted in the first case (the specific depends on it) and why the specific is the realizer in the second case (the general cannot be realized without it).
Al-Jurjani explains that the attribute is constituted by the described, but the differentia is not an attribute.
5
Review the commentator's clarifications
Note the nuance about 'rather the opposite' not meaning always, and that 'rational' is not an attribute but a realizer.
This prevents misunderstanding that the reversal is universal.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(Otherwise) i.e. if each does not apply to every individual of the other while they co-apply in some way, (then between them) necessarily (there is generality and specificity, either absolutely, and then either the general constitutes the specific), and this only occurs in mental essences like white body, for the intellect considers one essence from them, (or the general does not constitute the specific but rather the opposite), like rational animal, for 'rational' being a differentia is constitutive of 'animal' which is a genus, and like existent substance and existent quantity for example, for the more general here, i.e. 'existent', is an attribute of the more specific, contrary to white body, and there is no doubt that the attribute is constituted by the described absolutely. And as for 'rational', it is not an attribute of 'animal', but rather runs its course. His saying: (and this only occurs etc.) because the rank of constitution and realization is after the rank of being constituted, so the general is self-constituted and self-realized, and the specific subsists in it after its realization, so there is between them in external reality subsistence and inherence, and the composite of attribute and subject is only in the mind. His saying: (rather the opposite) he does not mean that it is absolutely the opposite, for it is possible that neither is constitutive of the other, but rather it is so in some cases, and he only added it to connect his saying: for 'rational' is constitutive of 'animal'. His saying: (and as for 'rational' etc.) because the attribute only subsists in the described after its realization, and 'animal' is not realized without 'rational'. His saying: (rather it runs its course) in terms of being applied to it and being its realizer, just as the attribute specifies the described.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage addresses the case where parts are not equal but have absolute generality and specificity. The author gives two subcases. First, the general constitutes the specific, as in 'white body': 'body' (the general) is constitutive of 'white' (the specific) because 'white' cannot exist independently but depends on a subject like body; the composite is mental (اعتبارية). Second, the specific constitutes the general, as in 'rational animal': 'rational' (a differentia) constitutes 'animal' (a genus) as its realizer. Also 'existent substance' and 'existent quantity' are examples where the general 'existent' is an attribute of the specific. The commentator explains the logical order: the general is self-constituted, then the specific subsists in it (in the first case), or the specific is the realizer of the general (in the second case). He also clarifies that the author's phrase 'rather the opposite' does not mean it always reverses, but only in some instances, and that 'rational' here acts as a realizer, not as an attribute.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The general always constitutes the specific.
Correction: In absolute generality, the specific can also constitute the general, as shown in 'rational animal' where the differentia 'rational' constitutes the genus 'animal'.

Key Takeaway
Absolute generality involves two possible relations of constitution: either the general constitutes the specific (as in mental essences like white body) or the specific constitutes the general (as in rational animal).

Generality from One Aspect: The Example of White Animal
Al-Jurjani explains generality from one aspect with the example of white animal as a mental essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the concept of generality from one aspect and why the composite 'white animal' is considered a mental essence rather than a real essence.

Core Idea
Some universal concepts overlap partially, each including what the other excludes.

In the study of universals, not all composite terms refer to real essences. Al-Jurjani introduces the notion of 'generality from one aspect' with the example of 'white animal'. This composite is a mental essence because its two components are not necessarily joined in reality. This lesson unpacks why such composites require mental consideration and what this teaches about logical classification.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the author's explanation of 'white animal' as a mental essence sink in.
Notice that the passage contrasts mental essence with real essence.
2
Understand mental vs real essence
Focus on the phrase 'mental essence' (ماهية اعتبارية). Ask yourself: why does Al-Jurjani say this composite is not a real essence? The answer lies in the composition requirement for real essences (genus + differentia).
Real essences are composed from genus and differentia without need for mental consideration.
3
Follow the reasoning about white animal
Trace why 'white animal' is not a real essence: animal and white are not genera and differentiae; each has individuals the other excludes. Therefore, their combination is merely mental.
The example shows that generality from one aspect occurs when each term applies to something the other does not.
4
Note the role of genus and differentia
The passage refers to the well-known view that real essences have only intellectual composition from genus and differentia (or equals). White animal lacks such a real composition.
This builds on earlier logical principles.
5
Reflect on the significance for classification
Consider why this distinction matters: it prevents us from treating accidental composites as real natural kinds. This refines our understanding of universals and their relationships.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإما من وجه نحو الحيوان الأبيض فإنه ماهية اعتبارية; لأن الماهية الحقيقية على ما هو المشهور فقد التأم ماهية منهما بلا احتياج إلى اعتبار معتبر. قوله : ( لأن الماهية الحقيقية إلخ) بناء على أن لا تركيب عقلياً للماهية الحقيقية إلا من الجنس والفصل، أو من متساويين. على ما هو المشهور، فقد التأم ماهية منهما بلا احتياج إلى اعتبار معتبر قلت : أراد بالماهية الماهية المركبة منهما فقط كما يتبادر من السياق، وأيضاً قد تقرر أن المقوم للحيوان أحدهما، وإنما ذكرا معاً في تعريفه لعدم العلم بأن أيهما متقدم مقوم له، فثبت الاحتياج إلى الاعتبار على كل تقدير . قوله : ( نحو الجسم الأبيض المتكلمون لا يقولون بالسطح، وحينئذ يظهر كون الجسم أعم مطلقاً من الأبيض، وهو ظاهر. ثم الجسم مقوم له أي معين ومحصل ؛ لأن الأبيض ليس له تحصل في نفسه بل في ضمن نوع كالجسم . قوله : (بل يكون الأمر بالعكس وهو على قسمين قسم يكون العام فيه جارياً مجرى الموصوف، والخاص مجرى الصفة، وقسم على العكس، فمثل المصنف للأول والشارح للثاني . قوله : (هو المقوم للحيوان أي المعين والمحصل له لا الداخل في قوامه كما هو في المشهور من معنى المقوم ومن هاهنا يقال فصل النوع مقوم له مقسم للجنس.
And (generality) from one aspect is like white animal, for it is a mental essence; because the real essence according to the well-known (view) is composed from them without need for a considering intellect. His saying: (because the real essence etc.) is based on there being no intellectual composition of the real essence except from genus and differentia, or from equals. According to the well-known (view), the essence is composed from them without need for a considering intellect. I say: He meant by 'essence' the composite essence from them only, as is apparent from the context, and also it has been established that the constitutive of animal is one of them, and they were mentioned together in its definition due to ignorance of which of them is prior and constitutive, so the need for consideration is established in every case. His saying: (like white body the theologians do not say with surface, and then it appears that body is absolutely more general than white, which is apparent. Then body is constitutive of it, i.e., determinant and realizer; because white has no realization in itself but within a species like body. His saying: (rather the opposite is on two divisions: a division where the general runs the course of the described and the specific runs the course of the attribute, and a division vice versa. So the author gave an example of the first, and the commentator gave an example of the second. His saying: (he is constitutive of animal) i.e., the determinant and realizer for it, not the one entering into its constitution as in the well-known meaning of constitutive, and from here it is said: the specific differentia is constitutive of it and divisive of the genus.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage explains the second type of generality and specificity: generality from one aspect (عموم من وجه). The example is 'white animal' (حيوان أبيض), where 'animal' and 'white' are general from one aspect because each applies to some individuals the other does not (e.g., non-white animals and white non-animals). Such a composite is considered a mental essence (ماهية اعتبارية) because the real essence (ماهية حقيقية) according to the well-known view does not require mental consideration for composition—it is composed from genus and differentia or from equals. But here the composition is not real but mental. The commentator elaborates on the concept of 'real essence' and why this example requires mental consideration, clarifying that the constitutive element is unknown, so both parts are mentioned together in definition. He also compares with 'white body' and reiterates the two divisions of the opposite case (general as attribute vs. specific as attribute), warning that the term 'constitutive' here means 'determinant and realizer' (معين ومحصل) not 'entering into the essence' (داخل في القوام).

Misconception: All composites of two universals are real essences if the parts are real.
Correction: Only composites where one part is genus and the other is differentia (or equals) are real essences; accidental combinations like 'white animal' are mental essences.

Key Takeaway
The composite 'white animal' is a mental essence because its parts are not necessarily constitutive of a single real kind; this illustrates the logical relation of generality from one aspect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Think of a belief you hold that combines ideas (e.g., 'successful person' or 'good Muslim')—have you ever mistaken that composite for a fixed reality? What would it mean to admit that such composites are constructs of the mind and not absolute truths?

Two Types of Generality-Specificity in Parts
Classification of generality-specificity types with examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main types of generality-specificity relations between parts—absolute and from a certain aspect—and the two subtypes of absolute where either the general constitutes the specific or vice versa.

Core Idea
Generality and specificity in parts can be absolute or aspectual.

This passage from the text introduces the classification of relations between parts when they do not completely cover each other. It distinguishes between absolute generality-specificity and that from a certain aspect, and within absolute, it identifies two cases depending on whether the general term constitutes the specific or the specific constitutes the general. The examples provided clarify the distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once without analyzing. Notice the author's structure of classification.
Pay attention to the phrases 'إما مطلقاً' and 'إما من وجه'.
2
Identify the two main types
Find where the author mentions absolute generality-specificity and then the subtype from a certain aspect.
The absolute type is further divided into two subcases.
3
Analyze the examples
Observe the examples of 'white body' and 'rational animal' and understand why each represents a different subtype.
White body is a mental quiddity; rational animal involves a real differentia.

The Second Observation - The Fifth Purpose: On the Division of Parts. ...from them, (And if not) i.e., if each does not apply to all individuals of the other while they are in agreement in general, (then between them) inevitably (there is generality and specificity, either absolute, and then either the general composes the specific), and this only occurs in mental quiddities like 'white body', for the intellect considers from them one quiddity, (or) the general does not compose the specific but rather the opposite, (like 'rational animal', for 'rational' being a differentia is the constituent of 'animal' which is a genus, and like 'existing substance' and 'existing quantity' for example, for the more general here, namely 'existing', is an attribute of the more specific, contrary to 'white body', and there is no doubt that the attribute is constituted by the described absolutely. But 'rational' is not an attribute of 'animal', rather it runs its course. (And from a certain aspect), a counterpart to his saying 'either absolutely' like 'white animal', for it is a mental quiddity; because the real quiddity... His statement: ('And this only occurs, etc.') because the rank of constituting and actualizing is after the rank of being constituted, so the general is constituted and actualized by itself, and the specific subsists by it after its actualization, so between them in external reality there is subsistence and accidentality, and the composite of accident and subject is only in the mind. His statement: ('Rather the opposite is the case') he does not mean that the opposite is absolutely the case, for it is possible that neither of them constitutes the other, but rather it is so in general, and he added it only to connect his statement: 'for rational is the constituent of animal'. His statement: ('As for rational, etc.') because the attribute only subsists by the described after its actualization, and animal is not actualized without rational. His statement: ('Rather it runs its course') considering it as predicated on it and as an actualizer for it, just as the attribute specifies the described. His statement: ('Because the real quiddity, etc.') based on the fact that there is no intellectual composition of the real quiddity except from genus and differentia, or from two coextensive terms. According to what is well-known, a quiddity composed of them is consolidated without need for a considering consideration. I said: He meant by 'quiddity' the quiddity composed of them only, as is evident from context, and also it has been established that the constituent of 'animal' is one of them, and they are mentioned together in its definition due to ignorance of which is prior and constituent, so the need for consideration is established in any case. His statement: ('Like 'white body'; the theologians do not speak of the surface, and then it is clear that 'body' is absolutely more general than 'white', and that is obvious. Then 'body' is its constituent, i.e., determiner and actualizer, because 'white' has no actuality by itself but only within a type like 'body'. His statement: ('Rather the opposite is the case') and it is of two divisions: a division where the general takes the role of the described and the specific takes the role of the attribute, and a division the opposite; the author gave an example of the first and the commentator of the second. His statement: ('It is the constituent of 'animal'') i.e., the determiner and actualizer for it, not the one entering into its constitution as is well-known in the meaning of 'constituent', and hence it is said that the differentia of a species is a constituent for it and a divider of the genus.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the relations between parts (اجزاء) in terms of generality and specificity (عموم وخصوص). The author explains that when two things do not apply to all individuals of each other but coincide in some cases, they have a relationship of generality and specificity. This is divided into two types: absolute (مطلقاً) and from a certain aspect (من وجه). In the absolute type, either the general constitutes the specific (يقوم العام الخاص) or vice versa. The first case (general constitutes specific) occurs in mental quiddities like 'white body': the intellect considers the two as one quiddity composed of general and specific. The second case (specific constitutes general) is exemplified by 'rational animal': here 'rational' (the specific) is the differentia that constitutes the genus 'animal', and similarly 'existing substance' where 'existence' is an attribute of the substance. The author then clarifies that 'rational' is not an attribute but a constituent. The commentary explains the reasoning: in the first case, the general is self-constituting and the specific subsists by it; in the second case, the specific is the actualizer. The passage also discusses the case of generality from a certain aspect, giving the example 'white animal' as a mental quiddity because the real quiddity has no intellectual composition except from genus and differentia or coextensive terms. The commentary adds that the examples given by the author and the commentator illustrate the two subcases: in one the general functions as the subject (موصوف) and the specific as the attribute, and in the other the roles are reversed. The key point is that the term 'constituent' (مقوم) here means determiner and actualizer, not component in the usual sense.

Key Takeaway
Generality-specificity in parts is classified as absolute or aspectual, with absolute further divided based on whether the general or the specific constitutes the other.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The lesson shows that some quiddities are mental constructions (like 'white animal') while others are real (like 'human'). When you define yourself by labels (e.g., 'successful', 'pious'), are you constructing a mental quiddity that may not reflect reality? Will you check whether your self-descriptions are absolute or aspectual?
?
How often do you mistake a partial overlap (generality from an aspect) for a complete identity? Commit to one change in how you examine relationships between concepts to avoid this error.
