Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that any statement like 'Humanity is not humanity' is always a logical contradiction and therefore impossible.
Correction: Al-Jurjani shows that a simple negation (e.g., 'Humanity is not humanity in external reality') is not contradictory if the subject does not exist. Only the modified positive form (e.g., 'Humanity is not-humanity') is impossible because it assumes the subject exists while being negated of itself.

Checkpoint
According to Al-Jurjani, what is the impossible judgment when the maker's act is absent?
A) The simple negative judgment (saliba kharijiyya)
B) The modified positive judgment (ījāb maʿdūl)
C) Both A and B are impossible
D) Neither is impossible

Key Takeaway
The core refutation hinges on distinguishing a simple negation (permissible) from a modified positive judgment (impossible).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you deny yourself something (like a sin or a habit), do you truly negate its existence in your life, or do you merely modify your relationship with it, keeping it present? How does Al-Jurjani's distinction challenge you to examine the sincerity of your own 'negations'?
?
In your moments of spiritual struggle, do you ever treat your failures as impossible to overcome because you think you must be perfect? How does the lesson that simple negation is permissible (when the subject is absent) help you accept your present shortcomings without despair?
?
Consider a time you claimed 'I am not this' (e.g., 'I am not angry'). Was that a simple negation (true because the anger was absent) or a modified positive judgment (denying while still harboring it)? How can you apply Al-Jurjani's logic to purify your intentions?

Logic and Technical Terms
advanced
8 min
Further Refinements: The Analogy, External Judgment, and the Mental Proposition Objection
Al-Jurjani further defends his refutation by clarifying the analogy and addressing objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Jurjani further defends his position by clarifying the analogy with non-existents, the nature of the external negative judgment, and responding to the objection that the proposition is mental.

Core Idea
Al-Jurjani shows that the external negative judgment here is not about individuals but about the external container, and the final objection about mental propositions is addressed by distinguishing between essence in actual fact and essence in external reality.

Having established the key distinction between simple negation and modified positive judgment, Al-Jurjani now addresses several remaining issues. He explains the analogy with non-existents, clarifies that the external negative judgment is not the conventional one, and finally tackles a counter-objection that the proposition 'Humanity is humanity' is mental, so its negation should be due to absence in the mind, not in external reality. This lesson unpacks these subtle points.

Guided Path
1
Examine the discussion about considering the absence alone
Read the first part where Al-Jurjani discusses that when we consider the absence of making alone, we judge that it entails impossibility, but this is not because it actually does; it is because we are not considering other factors. Understand the nuance about 'impossible in itself'.
2
Clarify the analogy with the non-existent
Al-Jurjani explains that the premise about the non-existent is an analogy: just as the non-existent is negated of itself, so essences without making are negated. He also notes that 'external' here means actual state of affairs (nafs al-amr). Pay attention to how he defends the analogy.
3
Understand the definition of external negative judgment
The passage clarifies that by 'external negative judgment' he does not mean the conventional one about individuals, but a judgment where external reality is the container. He also addresses a possible confusion about the correlation between the negative and the positive with negated predicate.
4
Analyze the final counter-objection
The last section presents a challenge: the proposition 'Humanity is humanity' is mental, so its negation should be due to absence in the mind, not in external reality. Al-Jurjani presents both sides: the proponent of made-ness says essence in actual fact depends on the maker's act, while the opponent argues that if it were made, it would not be itself in actual fact. He ends with 'fa-taʾammal' (so consider). Try to identify the unresolved point.

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection against the view that essences are made (majʿūla). The objection claims that if the maker's act is absent, the essence would cease to exist, and then a statement like 'Humanity is not humanity' would be impossible, implying the impossibility of the entire scenario. Al-Jurjani's response begins by distinguishing two types of judgments: a simple negative (saliba) and a positive judgment with a negated predicate (ījāb maʿdūl). The simple negative 'Humanity is not humanity in external reality' is true when the subject does not exist in external reality—since there is no subject to bear a positive attribute. This is not impossible. The impossible is only the positive judgment with a negated predicate, e.g., 'Humanity is not-humanity', which would require the subject to exist while being negated of itself. The refutation hinges on this logical distinction. Al-Jurjani then explains that when making is absent, the essence is entirely removed from external reality, so only negations are true. He addresses various objections: (1) that considering the absence of making alone might seem to entail impossibility, but this is only because we judge it to entail impossibility, not because it actually does; (2) that the analogy with the non-existent is valid because essences without making are like non-existents; (3) that the 'external negative judgment' here refers to a judgment where external reality is the container, not the conventional one about individuals; (4) that a positive judgment with a negated predicate would require the subject to exist, which is not the case. Finally, a counter-objection is raised: the proposition 'Humanity is humanity' is mental, so its negation's truth should be due to absence in the mind, not in external reality. The proponent of made-ness holds that humanity's being humanity in actual fact depends on the maker's act, while the opponent argues that if humanity were made, it would not be humanity without the act. Al-Jurjani suggests caution (fa-taʾammal) but concludes that the only impossible is the modified positive judgment, not the simple negation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the external negative judgment defined here is the same as the conventional one, leading to confusion about the absence of individuals.
Correction: Al-Jurjani explicitly states that here 'external' does not mean the conventional one about individuals; it means that external reality is the container of the judgment. The absence is of the subject itself, not of its individuals.

Checkpoint
According to Al-Jurjani, what does the 'external' in 'external negative judgment' mean in this context?
A) The judgment is about individuals existing in external reality
B) The judgment is about actual individuals
C) External reality is the container (ẓarf) of the judgment
D) The judgment is about mental existence

Key Takeaway
Al-Jurjani's refutation involves multiple layers: clarifying the analogy, redefining the type of judgment, and engaging with the objection that the proposition is mental, leaving the final point open for consideration.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Objection and Answer on the One Not by Individual
Al-Jurjani presents an objection and answer regarding the one not by individual.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection against classifying the 'one not by individual' as a type of the one, and how the answer defends its classification by distinguishing between two kinds of multiplicity.

Core Idea
The 'one not by individual' is one in predication, even if divisible into parts.

This passage presents a critical objection and its answer concerning the division of 'the one' in kalam. The objection argues that the 'one not by individual' is fundamentally many, so it cannot be a true one. The answer refines our understanding by showing that its unity lies in being a universal predicable of many, not in being indivisible. We will follow the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Understand the claim: the one not by individual accepts division into parts, so it is a locus for both unity and multiplicity. Therefore, it should not be a division of the one.
Notice the conditions: division can be essential/accidental, into similar/dissimilar parts.
2
Examine the answer
The answer states that the multiplicity of the one not by individual is only in being true of many individuals (ṣidq ʿalā kathīrīn), not in division into parts. This preserves its unity.
The contrasted counterpart is the one by individual, which is not true of many.
3
Note the additional clarification
The answer allows that the one not by individual may have another kind of multiplicity: division into quantitative or mental parts. However, this does not affect its unity as a universal.
Quantitative parts are physical; mental parts are conceptual.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The objection is resolved: the one not by individual remains a valid division of the one because its multiplicity is in predication, not in partitive division.
This illustrates the precision of kalam terminology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وهو الواحد بالاجتماع هاهنا بحث وهو أن الكلام في الواحد الذي ليس معروضاً للكثرة من جهة أخرى كما ينبئ عنه قوله : في الواحد لا بالشخص، وأنه كثير له جهة وحدة، فلا يجوز أن يجعل من أقسامه ما يقبل القسمة سواء كان قبولها لذاته أو لا لذاته، وسواء كانت القسمة إلى أجزاء متشابهة أو غير متشابهة لأن الواحد القابل للقسمة إلى الأجزاء معروض للوحدة والكثرة معاً من جهتين، لا سيما إذا كان الانقسام حاصلاً بالفعل، والوحدة اجتماعية، وجوابه : أن الواحد لا بالشخص جهة كثرته صدقه على كثيرين ويقابله الواحد بالشخص، وهو الذي لا يكون صادقاً على كثيرين فلا يكون له جهة كثرة على ذلك الوجه المخصوص، أعني الانقسام إلى الجزئيات ويجوز أن يكون له جهة كثرة على وجه آخر وهو الانقسام إلى الأجزاء المقدارية أو الذهنية.
He said: (It is the one by aggregation) here there is an investigation: namely, the discussion concerns the one that is not a locus for multiplicity from another aspect, as is indicated by his saying: regarding the one not by individual, and that it is many having an aspect of unity. So it is not permissible to make part of its divisions that which accepts division, whether its acceptance is essential or accidental, and whether the division is into similar or dissimilar parts, because the one that accepts division into parts is a locus for both unity and multiplicity from two aspects, especially if the division is actual and the unity is aggregative. And its answer: the one not by individual has its aspect of multiplicity in its being true of many individuals, and it is opposed by the one by individual, which is not true of many, so it does not have an aspect of multiplicity in that particular manner, namely division into particulars, but it may have an aspect of multiplicity in another manner, namely division into quantitative or mental parts.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer regarding the classification of the one. The objection argues that the 'one not by individual' (al-wāḥid lā bi al-shakhṣ) is actually many having an aspect of unity, so it should not be classified as a type of the one because it accepts division into parts. The objector claims that any one that is divisible (whether essentially or accidentally, into similar or dissimilar parts) is a locus for both unity and multiplicity, thus it cannot be considered truly one. The answer clarifies that 'one not by individual' has its multiplicity only in being predicable of many individuals (i.e., it is a universal), not in being divided into parts. The contrasted counterpart is the 'one by individual' (al-wāḥid bi al-shakhṣ), which is not predicable of many. The answer allows that a 'one not by individual' may indeed have another aspect of multiplicity, namely division into quantitative or mental parts, but that does not negate its being one in the sense of being a universal. Therefore, the classification stands.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The 'one not by individual' cannot be divided at all.
Correction: It can be divided into quantitative or mental parts, but its unity is in being a universal predicable of many, not in being indivisible.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The 'one not by individual' remains a valid division of the one because its multiplicity is only in predication, not in division into parts.

?
The objector thinks that if something is divisible, it cannot be truly one. In your own understanding of Islamic teachings, do you ever dismiss a comprehensive concept (like ihsan or taqwa) because it has multiple facets? When Objections Target Claims, Not Proofs
Commentator clarifies objection’s target and critiques an answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a kalam commentator clarifies the target of an objection and identifies a weakness in a proposed answer.

Core Idea
An objection to the claim itself changes the entire shape of the debate.

This short passage from the commentary unpacks a crucial nuance in the earlier objection. Before moving on, the commentator clarifies that the objection’s wording targets the claim itself (mad‘ā) not the proof (dalīl), and then points out a further problem with the proposed answer. Understanding this meta-commentary sharpens one’s ability to follow complex kalam arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let the commentator's own flow come through.
Notice the two distinct parts: first a clarification, then a further objection.
2
Identify the two parts of the commentary
The first part explains what 'what you mentioned' refers to. The second part questions the answer about made existence. Find the key terms: mudda‘ā, dalīl, mulāzama, wujūd maj‘ūl, takalluf.
3
Reflect on 'takalluf' and 'ta’ammal'
The commentator says the answer can be given with takalluf (difficulty) and then says 'so consider.' What does this suggest about the strength of the answer?
Takalluf implies a strained or forced explanation; the commentator is signaling doubt.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: لو صح ما ذكرتم المراد بما ذكرتم هو المدعى لا الدليل؛ ليكون الاعتراض معارضة، والملازمة المذكورة في المتن تفصيل الملازمة المذكورة في الشرح، وفائدة ذكرها ظهور توجيه الاعتراض.

قوله أو وجودها فيه نظر لأن الوجود المجعول يمكن أن يعتبر بالنسبة إلى البسائط أيضاً ، فما الفارق حينئذ؟ يمكن أن يجاب بالتكلف، فتأمل .
He said: If what you mentioned were correct, what is meant by 'what you mentioned' is the claim (al-muddaʿā), not the proof (al-dalīl); so that the objection would be a contradiction. And the implication (mulāzama) mentioned in the matn is a detail of the implication mentioned in the sharḥ, and the benefit of mentioning it is to show the direction of the objection. He said: Or its existence (wujūduhā) is questionable, because the made existence can be considered in relation to simples as well, so what is the difference then? It can be answered with difficulty (takalluf); so consider.
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage is a final commentary on the previous objection. It explains that the phrase 'what you mentioned' in the objection refers to the claim itself (that simples are not made) rather than the proof, so that the objection is a direct contradiction. It also notes a problem with the answer that 'the made thing is the existence of the composite': because the made existence can also be considered in relation to the simples, so what distinguishes the composite? The commentator suggests that this could be answered with difficulty (bi al-takalluf) and advises the reader to reflect (taʾammal).

Correction: The commentator explicitly states that it refers to the claim, so the objection is a direct contradiction.

Types of the One and the Priority of Oneness
Defines one by accident and one by relation, then argues which division is more entitled to oneness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two types of 'one by accident' and 'one by relation', and how the term 'one' is used by analogy across the divisions of person, species, genus, and differentia, with the person being most entitled to oneness.

Core Idea
The term 'one' is said by analogy; the one by person is the truest oneness.

In this passage, Sanusi continues his discussion of the one (al-wahid). He defines two specific types: one by accident (al-wahid bi al-mahmul) and one by relation (al-wahid bi al-nisba). He then explains that the word 'one' applies to its divisions by analogy (tashkik), and argues that the one by person is most deserving of the name 'one' because it has the least multiplicity. The passage also illustrates the relationship between genus, species, and differentia, using the example of evenness for four.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the structure: first definitions, then the comparison of degrees.
The passage starts mid-sentence; it continues from a previous discussion.
2
Identify the two types defined
Look for the definitions of 'one by accident' and 'one by relation'. Understand how each is different from the other types mentioned earlier.
One by accident involves a shared predicate; one by relation involves a comparison of relations.
3
Understand the analogy (tashkik)
The passage states that 'one' is said by analogy across its divisions. Consider why the one by person is the most entitled to oneness, followed by species, genus, and differentia.
The order is based on how much multiplicity is involved.
4
Examine the example of evenness
The example of evenness for four shows a necessary concomitant that attaches to quiddity. Reflect on how this illustrates the relationship between quiddity and accidents.
This example supports the discussion of essential vs. accidental unity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
محمول عليهما خارج عن ماهيتهما وهو موضوع لهما بالطبع، (أو) واحد ( بالمحمول ) إن كانت جهة الوحدة محمولة بالطبع على تلك الكثرة، ( كما يقال : القطن والثلج واحد في البياض، فإن الأبيض محمول عليهما طبعاً وخارج عنهما، ( أولا ) أي لا تكون جهة الوحدة ذاتية للكثرة ولا أمراً عرضياً لها، وذلك بأن لا تكون محمولة عليها أصلاً ( كما يقال : نسبة النفس إلى البدن هو نسبة الملك إلى المدينة)، ومعناه : أن للنفس تعلقاً خاصاً بالبدن بحسبه تتمكن من تدبيره والتصرف فيه دون غيره من الأبدان، وكذا للملك تعلق خاص بمدينته وبحسب ذلك يدبرها ويتصرف فيها دون غيرها من المدائن، فهذان التعلقان نسبتان متحدتان في التدبير الذي ليس مقوماً ولا عارضاً لشيء منهما، بل هو عارض للنفس والملك؛ فإن المدبر إنما يطلق حقيقة عليهما، وإذا اعتبرت الوحدة بين النفس والملك في التدبير، كانت من قبيل الاتحاد في العارض المحمول كاتحاد القطن والثلج في البياض، وإن اعتبرت بين النسبتين في كونهما نسبة كانت جهة الوحدة حينئذ إما مقومة لجهة الكثرة، أو عارضة لها. وإن اعتبر اتحاد النسبتين في كونهما منشأ للتدبير مثلاً كان ذلك اتحاداً في العارض المحمول . ( وقد يسمى ( الواحد الذي ليس جهة الوحدة فيه ذاتية ولا عرضية للكثرة (الواحد بالنسبة وأنت تعلم أن قول الواحد على هذه الأقسام المذكورة إنما هو ( بالتشكيك و تعلم (أيها ) أي أي هذه الأقسام أولى) بمعنى الوحدة من غيره إذ لا شك أن الواحدة بالشخص أولى بالوحدة من الواحد بالنوع، وهو أولى من الواحد بالجنس، الذي هو أولى من الواحد بالفصل، لأن جنس الشيء ماهية له مقولة عليه في جواب ما هو بحسب الشركة دون الفصل والواحد، فأينما وجدت الماهية كانت متصفة به ( وذلك كالزوجية للأربعة ) فإنها لازمة لماهية الأربعة وعارضة لها سواء وجدت الأربعة في الخارج أو في الذهن (فلو فرض أربعة ) موجودة بأحد الوجودين ( غير زوج لم تكن أربعة ) فيلزم التناقض، وكذا
It is predicated on both, being outside their quiddity, and it is a subject for them by nature. Or one by accident, if the aspect of unity is naturally predicated on that multiplicity, as is said: cotton and snow are one in whiteness, for white is naturally predicated on them and outside them. Or first: i.e., the aspect of unity is neither essential to the multiplicity nor accidental to it, such that it is not predicated on it at all, as is said: the relation of the soul to the body is like the relation of the king to the city. Its meaning: the soul has a particular attachment to the body by which it can manage and dispose of it without other bodies, and likewise the king has a particular attachment to his city by which he manages and disposes of it without other cities. These two attachments are two relations united in the management which is neither constitutive nor accidental to either of them; rather it is accidental to the soul and the king, for 'manager' is truly applied to them. If unity is considered between the soul and the king in management, it is of the type of union in the accidental predicated, like the union of cotton and snow in whiteness. If unity is considered between the two relations in being a relation, then the aspect of unity is either constitutive of the aspect of multiplicity or accidental to it. If the union of the two relations in being a source of management, for example, is considered, that is a union in the accidental predicated. (And sometimes) the one whose aspect of unity is neither essential nor accidental to the multiplicity is called (the one by relation). And you know that the term 'one' with respect to these mentioned divisions is only (by analogy). And you know (which of these divisions is more entitled) to the meaning of unity than others. For there is no doubt that the one by person is more entitled to unity than the one by species, and it is more entitled than the one by genus, which is more entitled than the one by differentia, because the genus of a thing is a quiddity for it predicated on it in answer to 'what is it?' according to commonality, unlike the differentia and the one. So wherever the quiddity is found, it is characterized by it. (Such as evenness for four), for it is necessary for the quiddity of four and accidental to it, whether four is found externally or in the mind. (If four) existing in either of the two modes of existence (were assumed) not even, (it would not be four), so contradiction would follow. And likewise.
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage defines two types of the one (al-wahid): one by accident (al-wahid bi al-mahmul) and one by relation (al-wahid bi al-nisba). It first explains that when two things share a common predicate that is outside their quiddity, they are one by accident, like cotton and snow both being white. Then it explains one by relation, where the unity is neither essential nor accidental to the multiplicity, like the relation of the soul to the body and of a king to his city. The passage then discusses the notion of 'one' being said by analogy (tashkik) across its various divisions: one by person, species, genus, and differentia. It argues that the one by person is most entitled to the meaning of oneness because it lacks multiplicity, followed by the one by species, then genus, then differentia. The reasoning is that the genus is more essential than the differentia because it answers the 'what is it?' question and is shared across many species. The example of evenness for four illustrates a necessary concomitant that attaches to quiddity. This passage is a classification and comparison of the degrees of unity.

Correction: The passage shows that 'one' is said by analogy (tashkik), meaning it applies with different degrees of priority and posteriority.

?

Three Divisions of Attributes Attaching to Quiddity
Classifies attributes into three types and concludes quiddity is not created.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three divisions of attributes that attach to quiddity and why quiddity itself is not considered created.

Core Idea
Attributes are not all the same: some are necessary, some attach to existence, some are mental constructs.

In this passage, the author presents a classification of the attributes that can attach to a quiddity (essence). This classification is crucial for understanding the relationship between quiddity and existence, and it leads to the important theological conclusion that quiddity itself is not created; rather, createdness is an accident of existence. Let's explore the three divisions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic original and the literal translation to get a sense of the author's precise terminology.
2
Identify the three divisions
The passage lists three types of attributes: necessary concomitants (lāzim), attributes of existence, and secondary intelligibles. Make sure you can distinguish them.
3
Understand the examples
For each division, notice the example given: triangle angles for necessary, finitude/temporality for existence, universality/particularity for secondary intelligibles.
4
Grasp the conclusion about quiddity
The passage ends with the scholars' statement that quiddity is not created, explaining that createdness attaches to identity (external existence), not quiddity itself.
5
Reflect on implications
Consider why this distinction matters for theology: it preserves the immutability of essences in God's knowledge while attributing creation to existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحال في تساوي الزوايا المثلث لقائمتين، فإنه لازم لماهية المثلث، وإن لم يكن بين الثبوت لها كالزوجية للأربعة، فلو تصور مثلث غير متساوي الزوايا لقائمتين لم يكن مثلثاً . ( وقسم آخر يلحق الوجود أي الهويات الخارجية، لا الماهية من حيث هي هي ( نحو التناهي والحدوث للجسم فإنه ) أي نحو ما ذكر ( لا يلزم ماهيته ) أي ماهية الجسم من حيث هي هي، بل وجوده الخارجي، فإن من تصور جسماً قديماً أو غير متناه لم يكن ذلك الشخص ( متناقضاً في نفسه، ولا متصوراً لجسم غير جسم كما لزمه ذلك في تصور أربعة غير زوج ( وقسم) ثالث يلحق الماهية (باعتبار وجودها في الذهن فيكون لخصوصية هذا الوجود مدخل في عروضه للماهية فلا يحاذى به أمر في الخارج وهذا القسم هو المسمى بالمعقولات الثانية، ( نحو الذاتية والعرضية والكلية والجزئية العارضة للأشياء الموجودة في الذهن، وليس في الخارج ما يطابقها . (فنبهوا) بقولهم أن الماهية غير مجعولة ) على أن المجعولية إنما تلحق الهوية لا الماهية)، أي هي من عوارض الوجود الخارجي، من عوارض الماهية من حيث هي هي. فلو تصور مثلاً (إنسان غير مجعول لم
The case is similar to the equality of the angles of a triangle to two right angles, for it is necessary for the quiddity of the triangle, even if it is not as firmly established for it as evenness for four. So if one imagines a triangle that is not equal in angles to two right angles, it would not be a triangle. (And another division) attaches to existence, i.e., external identities, not to the quiddity as such, such as finitude and temporality for body, for they do not attach to its quiddity, i.e., the quiddity of body as such, but to its external existence. For if one imagines a body that is eternal or infinite, that person is not contradictory in himself, nor is he imagining a body that is not a body, as he would be required to do in imagining four that is not even. (And a third division) attaches to the quiddity by virtue of its existence in the mind, so that the specificity of this existence has an input in its occurrence to the quiddity, so it does not correspond to anything external. This division is called the secondary intelligibles, such as essentiality, accidentally, universality, and particularity, which occur to things existing in the mind, and there is nothing in the external that corresponds to them. (So they pointed out) by their saying that quiddity is not created, that createdness only attaches to identity, not to quiddity, i.e., it is among the accidents of external existence, among the accidents of quiddity as such. So if one imagines, for example, a human that is not created, he has not...
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage classifies the attributes (lawaḥiq) that attach to quiddity into three divisions. First, necessary concomitants (lazim) like the equality of triangle angles to two right angles, which if removed would negate the quiddity. Second, attributes that attach to existence, not to quiddity as such, like finitude and temporality for body—one can conceive of an eternal body without contradiction. Third, attributes that attach to quiddity by virtue of its mental existence, called secondary intelligibles (al-ma'qulat al-thaniya), such as universality and particularity, which have no external counterpart. The passage concludes that when the scholars say 'quiddity is not created,' they mean that createdness is an accident of external existence, not a property of quiddity itself. The final sentence starts to give an example but is cut off in the text.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think quiddity can be created, i.e., that existence is a property of essence.
Correction: The passage clarifies that createdness is an accident of external existence, not of quiddity as such. Quiddity itself is not created.

Checkpoint

A) Evenness of four
B) Finitude of body
C) Universality

Quiddity itself is not created; createdness is an attribute of external existence, not of essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider an attribute you believe defines you (e.g., your nationality, profession, or personality trait). Is this attribute a necessary concomitant of your essence, or does it attach only through your external existence? What would you be if that attribute were removed? Be honest: could you still be essentially yourself?
?
The passage teaches that quiddity itself is not created. If your deepest essence is uncreated, then what does that mean for your accountability before Allah? Are your actions accidental to your existence or essential to your quiddity? Confront the possibility that your sins are not part of your essence—so why do you cling to them as if they define you?
?
You conceive of yourself as 'existing' in your own mind. How much of that self-concept is actually a secondary intelligible—a mental construct with no external counterpart? This week, catch yourself acting as if your thoughts about yourself are absolute reality. Ask: is this true of my quiddity, or is it just a mind-dependent attribute?

Why Possibility is a Secondary Intelligible
Commentary defending possibility as a secondary intelligible by distinguishing mental from absolute existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the reasoning behind classifying possibility (al-imkan) as a secondary intelligible and the role of mental existence in this classification.

Core Idea
Possibility is a mental consideration, not an external attribute.

This passage from the commentary defends why possibility (al-imkan) belongs to the category of secondary intelligibles. A potential objection is raised: since possibility is a necessary concomitant of quiddity, how can it be merely mental? The answer hinges on distinguishing between absolute existence and mental existence, showing that the receptivity to existence and non-existence is only considered in the mind.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without stopping to analyze every detail. Then read the translation to grasp the overall flow of the argument.
The passage contains some garbled phrases, but the main argument is clear.
2
Identify the objection about possibility
Look for the question: how can possibility be a secondary intelligible if it is a necessary concomitant of quiddity? Find the exact wording in the passage.
The objection is introduced with 'إن قلت الإمكان من المعقولات الثانية مع أنه لازم...'.
3
Understand the answer's distinction
Notice how the author distinguishes between absolute existence and mental existence. Possibility attaches to quiddity only by virtue of mental existence, not absolute existence.
This is the key move to resolve the objection.
4
Reflect on the regress objection
Consider the further objection about a regress of mental existences. The author refers to a previous discussion, indicating that mental existence is foundational.
This shows the depth of the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (لا) الماهية من حيث هي هي تأكيد لدفع ما يتراءى من ظاهر العبارة من أنها ليست عارضة للماهيات أصلا . قوله : ( فلا يحاذى به أمر في الخارج أي لا يطابقه على ما مر من تفسير المطابقة من أنه لو فرض الحاصل في الذهن متصفا بالعوارض الخارجية، كان عين ذلك الأمر، ولو فرض ذلك الأمر الخارجي حاصلاً في العقل معرى عنها كان عين تلك الصورة، فلا يرد ما قيل: إن الوجود وكذا المطلق يحاذى بهما أمر في الخارج على رأي الحكماء، أعني ذاته تعالى لكون وجوده عين ذاته، فلا يكونان من المعقولات الثانية . قول: (فلو تصور إلخ) الفاء للتعليل، أو للتفريع، ففيه إشارة إلى الفرق بين الزوجية الخارجي قوله : ( وقسم يلحق الماهية باعتبار وجودها في (الذهن الظاهر أن التناقض آت في لواحق الوجود الذهني أيضاً. للماهية كما قوله : (هو المسمى بالمعقولات (الثانية) إن قلت الإمكان من المعقولات الثانية مع أنه لازم قلت : معناه أنه لازم لموصوفه الذي هو الماهية الممكنة، لا باعتبار مطلق سيجيء، الوجود، بل باعتبار الوجود الذهني، فإن معنى إمكان الماهية هو قابلية الماهية للوجود والعدم من حيث هي وتلك القابلية والحيثية لا تعرض إلا بحسب الوجود الذهني، فإن قلت إمكان الوجود في الذهن أيضاً من المعقولات الثانية، مع أن ثبوته للماهية ليس باعتبار الوجود الذهني، وإلا تسلسل الوجودات الذهنية، وليست اعتبارية صرفة حتى يلتزم قلت سبق الكلام فيه في بحث الوجود فليتذكر .
His saying: '(No) to quiddity as such' is a confirmation to repel what appears from the apparent expression that they are not accidental to quiddities at all. His saying: 'So it does not correspond to anything external' i.e., it does not match it, according to what was earlier of the interpretation of correspondence, that if what is obtained in the mind is assumed to be characterized by external accidents, it would be the very same thing; and if that external thing were assumed to be obtained in the mind stripped of them, it would be the very same form. So it does not refute what is said: that existence and likewise the absolute correspond to something external according to the opinion of the philosophers, namely His essence, may He be exalted, because His existence is identical to His essence, so they are not among the secondary intelligibles. His saying: 'So if one imagines, etc.', the fa is for explanation or for branching, so it contains an indication of the difference between evenness... [garbled]. His saying: 'And a division attaches to quiddity by virtue of its existence in the mind' – it is apparent that contradiction also occurs in the concomitants of mental existence. For quiddity as well. His saying: 'It is called secondary intelligibles' – if you say: possibility is among the secondary intelligibles while it is a necessary concomitant... I say: its meaning is that it is necessary for its described thing which is the possible quiddity, not by virtue of absolute existence, as will come, but by virtue of mental existence. For the meaning of the possibility of quiddity is the quiddity's receptivity to existence and non-existence as such, and that receptivity and consideration only occur according to mental existence. If you say: the possibility of existence in the mind is also among the secondary intelligibles, while its affirmation to quiddity is not by virtue of mental existence, otherwise there would be a regress of mental existences, and they are not purely conceptual so as to be accepted. I say: the discussion on it preceded in the section on existence, so let it be remembered.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (qala) that explains and defends the classification of secondary intelligibles. It first clarifies that the phrase 'no to quiddity as such' is a confirmation, not a denial that these attributes are accidental to quiddities. Then it explains that secondary intelligibles do not correspond to any external reality, addressing a potential objection about existence and the absolute. It then addresses a specific objection: how can possibility (al-imkan) be a secondary intelligible when it is a necessary concomitant? The answer distinguishes between possibility relative to absolute existence and possibility relative to mental existence, clarifying that the receptivity to existence and non-existence is considered only in the mind. A further objection about regress is answered by reference to a previous discussion. The commentary thus shows how even necessary concomitants like possibility have a mental consideration.

Correction: Possibility is a mental consideration; it is a secondary intelligible that attaches to quiddity only in the mind, not in external reality.

The Three Kinds of Attributes Attaching to Quiddity
Jurjani classifies three kinds of attributes that attach to quiddity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three divisions of attributes that attach to quiddity and how they differ in terms of necessity, existence, and mental consideration.

Core Idea
Attributes attach to quiddity in three distinct ways: necessary, existential, or mental.

This passage by Jurjani introduces a key metaphysical classification in Islamic philosophy and kalam: the types of attributes (عوارض) that can be predicated of a quiddity. Understanding this distinction is crucial for analyzing arguments about essence and existence. Let's examine the three divisions as laid out in the text.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Jurjani uses a very structured classification with clear examples.
2
Identify the first division
Find the attributes that are necessary for the quiddity itself, such as the equality of angles for a triangle. Understand why they are inseparable from the quiddity.
These are sometimes called 'essential attributes' in later philosophy.
3
Identify the second division
Find the attributes that attach to external existence, not to quiddity as such, like finitude and temporality for body. Notice that imagining their opposite is not contradictory.
This shows that some properties belong to existence, not essence.
4
Identify the third division
Find the attributes that attach to quiddity due to its mental existence, called secondary intelligibles. Examples: essentiality, accidentally, universality, particularity.
These have no external counterpart; they arise from the mind's reflection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحال في تساوي زوايا المثلث لقائمتين، فإنه لازم لماهية المثلث، وإن لم يكن بين الثبوت لها كالزوجية للأربعة، فلو تصور مثلث غير متساوي الزوايا لقائمتين لم يكن مثلثاً . ( وقسم آخر يلحق الوجود أي الهويات الخارجية، لا الماهية من حيث هي هي ( نحو التناهي والحدوث للجسم فإنه ) أي نحو ما ذكر ( لا يلزم ماهيته ) أي ماهية الجسم من حيث هي هي، بل وجوده الخارجي، فإن من تصور جسماً قديماً أو غير متناه لم يكن ذلك الشخص ( متناقضاً في نفسه، ولا متصوراً لجسم غير جسم كما لزمه ذلك في تصور أربعة غير زوج ( وقسم) ثالث يلحق الماهية (باعتبار وجودها في الذهن فيكون لخصوصية هذا الوجود مدخل في عروضه للماهية فلا يحاذى به أمر في الخارج وهذا القسم هو المسمى بالمعقولات الثانية، ( نحو الذاتية والعرضية والكلية والجزئية العارضة للأشياء الموجودة في الذهن، وليس في الخارج ما يطابقها .
The state regarding the equality of the angles of the triangle to two right angles: it is necessary for the quiddity of the triangle, even if it is not [considered] between affirmation for it, like evenness for four. So if one imagines a triangle whose angles are not equal to two right angles, it would not be a triangle. And another division attaches to existence, i.e., external identities, not to quiddity insofar as it is itself, such as finitude and temporality for body – for it does not necessitate its quiddity, i.e., the quiddity of body insofar as it is itself, but rather its external existence. For if one imagines a body that is eternal or infinite, that person would not be contradictory in himself, nor would he be imagining a body that is not a body, as would be required in imagining four that is not even. And a third division attaches to quiddity by virtue of its existence in the mind, so that the specificity of this existence has a role in its occurrence to the quiddity, and thus nothing in the external world corresponds to it. This division is called the secondary intelligibles, such as essentiality, accidentally, universality, and particularity, which occur to things existing in the mind, and there is nothing in the external world that matches them.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage classifies the types of attributes (عوارض) that can attach to a quiddity (ماهية). The author identifies three divisions. The first division consists of attributes that are necessary for the quiddity itself, such as the equality of the angles of a triangle to two right angles. This attribute is inseparable from the quiddity of a triangle; if one imagines a triangle lacking this property, it ceases to be a triangle. The second division consists of attributes that attach to external existence, not to the quiddity insofar as it is itself. For example, finitude and temporality (حدوث) are necessary for the existence of a body in the external world, but they are not part of the quiddity of body as such. One can imagine a body as eternal or infinite without contradiction, because the quiddity of body does not include these properties. The third division consists of attributes that attach to quiddity by virtue of its mental existence. These are called secondary intelligibles (المعقولات الثانية), such as essentiality (ذاتية) and accidentally (عرضية). These attributes are not found in the external world; they arise specifically due to the mind's consideration of quiddities.

Key Terms
ماهية
عوارض
المعقولات الثانية
ذاتية
عرضية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that attributes like finitude are part of the quiddity of body.
Correction: The author clarifies that finitude and temporality belong to external existence, not to the quiddity of body as such.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of an attribute that attaches to quiddity itself?
A) Finitude for body
B) Equality of angles to two right angles for triangle
C) Universality for mental existence
D) Temporality for body

Key Takeaway
The three divisions show that not all attributes of a thing are part of its essence; some belong to its external existence, and others arise from mental consideration.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your own existence, do you tend to focus on necessary attributes (like being human) or on existential ones (like your age or location)? How does this classification challenge your usual way of understanding yourself?
?
The author says that imagining an eternal or infinite body is not contradictory. But your belief in God's eternity is based on revelation, not logic alone. How does this distinction between necessary and existential attributes help you articulate why God's attributes are different from those of created bodies?
?
The secondary intelligibles (like 'universality') exist only in the mind. Are there concepts you treat as objective realities that might actually be mental constructs? What would change if you examined them more critically?

Being-Made as a Secondary Intelligible: Objection and Response
Presents objection and response regarding being-made as secondary intelligible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between two meanings of 'being-made' (al-juc'uliyya) and how it resolves the objection that being-made cannot be attributed externally if it is a secondary intelligible.

Core Idea
Being-made can be attributed externally when it is caused by need, not need itself.

This lesson examines a critical objection to the doctrine that essences are 'made' according to external existence. The objection claims that if being-made is a secondary intelligible, then its attribution is purely mental, not external. The response clarifies a crucial distinction that saves the doctrine.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the objection that being-made cannot be a secondary intelligible if it is to be attributed externally. Understand the claim that external existence is not the source of attribution.
The objection is attributed to al-Dawwani.
2
Identify the two meanings of 'being-made'
The response distinguishes between 'being-made' meaning the need itself (al-ihtiyaj) and 'being-made' as the effect caused by that need. Notice how this distinction addresses the objection.
3
Understand the response
When being-made is understood as the being-made caused by need, its attribution is according to external existence, not merely mental. This shows the initial objection was based on a misunderstanding.
Relate this to the earlier discussion of essences being made.
4
Reflect on the broader implications
Consider how this distinction fits into the classification of secondary intelligibles. Secondary intelligibles can have external considerations when they are grounded in real relations.
This is a key point in Islamic metaphysics.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( كان الكلام صحيحاً لا يخفى أن المعقولات الثانية ما يكون الذهن ظرفاً للاتصاف به، سواء كان ذلك المفهوم مقيداً بالخارج أو بالذهن، أو لم يكن مقيداً بهما. ولذلك جعلوا العلية ،والمعلولية والإمكان والحقيقة منها سواء اعتبر بحسب الوجود الخارجي أو غيره، بل جعلوا نفس الوجود الخارجي منها . والظاهر أن المجعولية بحسب الوجود الخارجي من المعقولات الثانية كيف لا وقد صرحوا بأن الإمكان علة الحاجة، فلا يكون منشأ الاتصاف بها الوجود الخارجي، فلا يكون الكلام على هذا التفسير صحيحاً، كذا أفاده المحقق الدواني. والجواب أن ذلك إنما يرد لو أريد بالمجعولية نفس الاحتياج على ما بوهمه ظاهر العبارة، أما إذا أريد بها المجعولية المسببة عن الاحتياج كما مر تقريره، فظاهر أن الاتصاف بها بحسب الوجود الخارجي. قوله : ( والتقييد تكلفاً إذ لا فائدة له، وهذا كما قال الزوجية الخارجية ليست لازمة لماهية الأربعة، بل لهويتها لا لعدم القرينة على التقييد حتى يرد أن كون المتبادر من الوجود الوجود الخارجي قرينة على التقييد المذكور، فلا تكلف فيه .
His statement: 'The speech would be correct' is not hidden (from you) that the secondary intelligibles are that for which the mind is the locus of attribution, whether that concept is restricted to the external or to the mind, or not restricted to either. Therefore they made causality, effecthood, contingency, and reality among them, whether considered according to external existence or otherwise; rather, they made even external existence among them. And it appears that being-made (al-juc'uliyya) according to external existence is among the secondary intelligibles; how could it not be, while they have stated that contingency is the cause of need, so the external existence is not the source of attribution to it, so the speech according to this interpretation is not correct. This is what the investigator al-Dawwani stated. And the answer is that this objection only arises if by being-made is meant the need itself, as the apparent meaning of the expression suggests. But if by it is meant the being-made that is caused by the need, as has been explained earlier, then it is evident that the attribution to it is according to external existence. His statement: 'And the restriction is unnecessary' since there is no benefit in it. And this is as it is said: External evenness is not necessary for the essence of four, rather for its identity, not due to the absence of a context for restriction, so that it might be objected that the understanding from existence is external existence, which is a context for the mentioned restriction, so there is no unnecessary restriction in it.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection concerning whether 'being-made' (al-juc'uliyya) is a secondary intelligible (al-ma'qulat al-thaniya). The objector claims that if being-made is a secondary intelligible, then the attribution of being-made is not based on external existence but on mental consideration, so the speech that essences are made according to external existence would be incorrect. The response distinguishes between two meanings of 'being-made': (1) the need for a maker, which is a secondary intelligible, and (2) the being-made that is caused by that need, which is attributed according to external existence. The passage then discusses the issue of 'restriction' (al-taqyid), arguing that if by 'existence' is understood external existence, then the restriction is not unnecessary, contrary to the claim. The example of 'evenness' (al-zawjiyya) is given: external evenness is not necessary for the essence of four but for its identity, so restricting the existence to the external is not without benefit.

الْمَجْعُولِيَّة
الاحْتِيَاج
الإمْكَان
الوُجُود الْخَارِجِي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Being-made is purely a mental concept with no external reference.
Correction: Being-made can be attributed externally when it is understood as the effect caused by a real need, as clarified in the passage.

Checkpoint
According to the answer given, when is the attribution of being-made (al-juc'uliyya) according to external existence?
A) When it is considered as a secondary intelligible.
B) When it is intended as the being-made caused by the need (al-ihtiyaj).
C) When it is intended as the need itself.
D) When it is restricted to the mind.

Key Takeaway
The distinction between need and being-made caused by need is essential for understanding how being-made can be attributed externally.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel a deep spiritual need—like the need for closeness to Allah—do you stop at merely acknowledging the need, or do you allow that need to cause you to actively seek being 'made' into what you should be? What holds you back from turning need into action?
?
Perhaps there is a situation where you have confused 'the feeling of need' with 'the actual being-made by Allah.' Are you mistaking your own awareness of a flaw for the actual divine reformation that should follow? What would it mean for you to let your awareness lead to a real change, not just a mental note?
?
The objection in the passage misinterprets being-made as the need itself. Where in your life might you be misinterpreting your own difficulties—seeing only the symptom (the hardship) and missing the deeper reality that Allah is using that hardship to make something new in you? What would it take to shift your perspective from seeing need as the end to seeing it as the cause of something greater?

The Benefit of Restricting Existence to the External
Argues that restricting existence to external is beneficial, using evenness example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that restricting the term 'existence' to external existence is not unnecessary, using the example of evenness in four.

Core Idea
Restriction is beneficial when it clarifies the intended meaning, as seen in the evenness example.

This lesson addresses a separate point: whether the restriction of 'existence' to external existence is unnecessary. Through the analogy of 'evenness' in the number four, the author demonstrates that such restriction has benefit and is not redundant.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection about restriction
The passage begins with the claim that restriction is an unnecessary formality because it provides no benefit.
This is a continuation of the discussion on being-made.
2
Analyze the evenness analogy
Consider the example: external evenness is not necessary for the essence of four but for its identity. This shows that restriction can be meaningful.
Evenness is a property that can be considered mentally or externally.
3
Connect to the issue of existence
If 'existence' is understood by default as external existence, then the restriction is not redundant; it clarifies what is meant.
The context (al-qarina) of the discussion indicates the intended meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( كان الكلام صحيحاً لا يخفى أن المعقولات الثانية ما يكون الذهن ظرفاً للاتصاف به، سواء كان ذلك المفهوم مقيداً بالخارج أو بالذهن، أو لم يكن مقيداً بهما. ولذلك جعلوا العلية ،والمعلولية والإمكان والحقيقة منها سواء اعتبر بحسب الوجود الخارجي أو غيره، بل جعلوا نفس الوجود الخارجي منها . والظاهر أن المجعولية بحسب الوجود الخارجي من المعقولات الثانية كيف لا وقد صرحوا بأن الإمكان علة الحاجة، فلا يكون منشأ الاتصاف بها الوجود الخارجي، فلا يكون الكلام على هذا التفسير صحيحاً، كذا أفاده المحقق الدواني. والجواب أن ذلك إنما يرد لو أريد بالمجعولية نفس الاحتياج على ما بوهمه ظاهر العبارة، أما إذا أريد بها المجعولية المسببة عن الاحتياج كما مر تقريره، فظاهر أن الاتصاف بها بحسب الوجود الخارجي. قوله : ( والتقييد تكلفاً إذ لا فائدة له، وهذا كما قال الزوجية الخارجية ليست لازمة لماهية الأربعة، بل لهويتها لا لعدم القرينة على التقييد حتى يرد أن كون المتبادر من الوجود الوجود الخارجي قرينة على التقييد المذكور، فلا تكلف فيه .
His statement: 'The speech would be correct' is not hidden (from you) that the secondary intelligibles are that for which the mind is the locus of attribution, whether that concept is restricted to the external or to the mind, or not restricted to either. Therefore they made causality, effecthood, contingency, and reality among them, whether considered according to external existence or otherwise; rather, they made even external existence among them. And it appears that being-made (al-juc'uliyya) according to external existence is among the secondary intelligibles; how could it not be, while they have stated that contingency is the cause of need, so the external existence is not the source of attribution to it, so the speech according to this interpretation is not correct. This is what the investigator al-Dawwani stated. And the answer is that this objection only arises if by being-made is meant the need itself, as the apparent meaning of the expression suggests. But if by it is meant the being-made that is caused by the need, as has been explained earlier, then it is evident that the attribution to it is according to external existence. His statement: 'And the restriction is unnecessary' since there is no benefit in it. And this is as it is said: External evenness is not necessary for the essence of four, rather for its identity, not due to the absence of a context for restriction, so that it might be objected that the understanding from existence is external existence, which is a context for the mentioned restriction, so there is no unnecessary restriction in it.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection concerning whether 'being-made' (al-juc'uliyya) is a secondary intelligible (al-ma'qulat al-thaniya). The objector claims that if being-made is a secondary intelligible, then the attribution of being-made is not based on external existence but on mental consideration, so the speech that essences are made according to external existence would be incorrect. The response distinguishes between two meanings of 'being-made': (1) the need for a maker, which is a secondary intelligible, and (2) the being-made that is caused by that need, which is attributed according to external existence. The passage then discusses the issue of 'restriction' (al-taqyid), arguing that if by 'existence' is understood external existence, then the restriction is not unnecessary, contrary to the claim. The example of 'evenness' (al-zawjiyya) is given: external evenness is not necessary for the essence of four but for its identity, so restricting the existence to the external is not without benefit.

Key Terms
التَّقْيِيد
الزَّوْجِيَّة الْخَارِجِيَّة
الْمَاهِيَّة
الْهَوِيَّة
الْقَرِينَة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Restricting a term always adds unnecessary complexity.
Correction: Restriction can be beneficial when it clarifies a specific intended meaning, especially when the term is ambiguous, as shown with 'existence' and 'evenness'.

Checkpoint
What does the example of evenness in four illustrate about restriction?
A) That restriction is always unnecessary.
B) That external evenness is necessary for the essence of four.
C) That restriction has benefit when context indicates a specific meaning.
D) That being-made is a secondary intelligible.

Key Takeaway
Restriction is not merely decorative; it serves to specify the intended meaning when context may be ambiguous, as illustrated by the evenness analogy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you assumed that your default understanding of a religious term (like 'faith' or 'mercy') was sufficient, only to later realize you missed a deeper, more specific meaning? How did that affect your practice?
?
The evenness example shows that what is necessary for essence versus identity can differ. In your own life, is your identity as a Muslim defined merely by external actions (like rituals) or by an internal essence? Where do you need to stop assuming the default and ask for specification?
?
The passage uses a logical restriction to avoid ambiguity. What is one belief you hold about God or creation that you have never questioned because it seemed 'obvious'? What would it cost you to examine it more precisely, and are you willing to do so today?

The Meaning of Making and the Objection
The commentator objects to the initial argument and clarifies the meaning of making.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the commentator's objection to the initial argument and his clarification of the meaning of influence and making.

Core Idea
Even without distinction, an essence can be an effect of the agent.

The commentator begins by critiquing the statement that 'since there is no distinction' between essence and existence, the essence cannot be made. He points out that this only refutes one sense of making. He then clarifies the true meaning of influence and the role of the second premise in explaining the full scope of making.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the commentator's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the structure: an objection followed by a clarification.
2
Identify the commentator's objection
Find where the commentator says the statement only proves non-attachment of making in one sense, not that the essence itself cannot be an effect.
The key phrase is 'لا يفيد نفي تعلق الجعل به بأن يكون نفسه أثر الفاعل'.
3
Understand the two senses of influence
The commentator distinguishes between influence as 'the effect following the agent' and the common notion of 'causing existence'. Ponder why this distinction matters.
4
Grasp the purpose of the second premise
The premise 'And likewise' is a prelude to explain the meaning of making and to refute the worry that if one essence is not made, then no essence is made.
The commentator says it has no role in proving that essences are not made.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (إذ لا مغايرة إلخ) فيه بحث؛ لأن هذا إنما يفيد عدم تعلق الجعل بالسواد بمعنى جعل شيء شيئاً؛ ولا يفيد نفي تعلق الجعل به بأن يكون نفسه أثر الفاعل وتابعاً للجعل، ومعنى التأثير استتباع المؤثر الأثر، لا ما يتبادر إلى الوهم، أعني إيجاد الأثر. قوله : ( وكذا إلخ ) هذه المقدمة لا دخل لها في بيان أنها ليست بمجعولة، بل توطئة لبيان معنى الجعل، ودفع لما مر من أنه إذا لم تكن ماهية ما مجعولة انتفى المجعولية بالكلية؛ لأن كل ما يفرض تعلق الجعل به من الوجود والموصوفية فهو ماهية في نفسه
His saying: (Since there is no distinction etc.) It contains discussion. Because this only proves the non-attachment of making to blackness in the sense of making a thing a thing; it does not prove the negation of the attachment of making to it by it being itself the effect of the agent and a consequence of making. And the meaning of influence is the effect following the agent, not what comes to mind, namely causing the effect to exist. His saying: (And likewise etc.) This premise has no role in explaining that they are not made, but rather it is a prelude to explaining the meaning of making, and a refutation of what previously passed that if a particular essence is not made, then made-ness is negated entirely, because everything to which making is attached, whether existence or being qualified, is itself an essence.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage is commentary on the matn's first statement. The commentator objects that the statement 'since there is no distinction' only proves that making in the sense of making a thing a thing is not applicable, but it does not deny that the essence itself can be the effect of the agent. He distinguishes between two senses of influence: one is the effect following the agent, not simply causing existence. Then he comments on the phrase 'And likewise' saying that this premise is not directly relevant to proving that essences are not made, but rather it prepares for the meaning of making and refutes the worry that if one essence is not made, then no essence is made, because everything that has making attached to it—existence or being qualified—is itself an essence.

Key Terms
تعلق الجعل
أثر
تأثير
استتباع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'distinction' is necessary for making, so if no distinction exists, then making is impossible.
Correction: The commentator clarifies that even without distinction, the essence can still be an effect of the agent, so making in the sense of being an effect is possible.

Checkpoint
According to the objection, what does the statement 'since there is no distinction' fail to prove?
A) That making is impossible
B) That the essence is not an effect of the agent
C) That the essence is self-subsistent
D) That existence is an accident

Key Takeaway
The commentator clarifies that the absence of distinction does not negate the possibility of the essence being an effect of the agent, and that influence means the effect's following upon the agent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own existence, do you see yourself merely as a thing among things, or as an effect of the Divine agent whose very essence follows from His act? How does this shift your understanding of your dependency on Him?
?
The commentator warns against reducing influence to mere causing existence. In your life, do you sometimes reduce your relationship with Allah to a transactional 'cause and effect', forgetting that your very being is an ongoing following from His will? What would change if you lived in constant awareness that you are an effect continuously following His agent?
?
If every essence, including yours, is not made in the sense of being turned from one thing to another, yet is still an effect of the agent, how does this reshape your gratitude and humility before your Creator? Can you name one area where you've acted as if your essence is self-sufficient?

Are Essences Made? The Mursad's Argument
Al-Iji argues that essences are not made in themselves but are made in being existent, using the dyer analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that essences are not made in themselves but are made in being existent, and why this distinction is not contradictory.

Core Idea
Essences are not made in themselves, but are made in being existent.

Al-Iji addresses a subtle theological question: Are essences themselves created? He argues that if an essence were simply itself, no making is conceivable. However, he introduces a second sense: the agent makes the essence existent, not the essence itself. The dyer analogy clarifies this distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation once to grasp the flow. Note the two different senses of 'making'.
This passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Understand the negative argument
Identify why making is inconceivable for an essence in itself: there is no distinction between essence and itself.
This uses the concept of مغايرة (distinction).
3
Understand the positive argument with the dyer analogy
See how the dyer makes the garment qualified by dye without making the garment itself. Apply this to God and essences.
The point is that making affects the qualification, not the essence itself.
4
Reconcile the two statements
The passage concludes that negating and affirming made-ness are both correct in different senses. Understand why there is no contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثاني - المقصد السادس : الماهيات هل هي مجعولة أم لا معها مفهوماً سواها، لم يعقل هناك جعل، إذ لا مغايرة بين الماهية ونفسها حتى يتصور توسط جعل جاعل بينهما، فتكون إحداهما مجعولة تلك الأخرى. وكذا لا يتصور تأثير الفاعل في الوجود بمعني جعل الوجود وجوداً ، بل تأثيره في الماهية باعتبار الوجود، بمعنى أنه يجعلها متصفة بالوجود، لا بمعنى أنه يجعل اتصافها موجوداً متحققاً في الخارج، فإن الصباغ مثلاً إذا صبغ ثوباً، فإنه لا يجعل الثوب ثوباً، ولا الصبغ صبغا، بل يجعل الثوب متصفا بالصبغ في الخارج وإن لم يجعل اتصافه به موجوداً ثابتاً في الخارج، فليست الماهيات في أنفسها مجعولةً، ولا وجوداتها أيضاً في أنفسها مجعولة، بل الماهيات في كونها موجودة مجعولة وهذا المعنى مما لا ينبغي أن ينازع فيه ولا منافاة بين نفي المجعولية عن الماهيات بالمعنى الذي ذكرناه أولاً، وبين إثباتها لها بما بينا آنفا أنه الحق الذي لا يتوهم بطلانه. فالقول بنفي المجعولية مطلقاً، وبإثباتها مطلقاً كلاهما صحيح ، إذا حملا على ما صورناه .
The Second Observatory - The Sixth Purpose: Are essences made or not? With it as a concept other than itself, no making is conceivable, since there is no distinction between the essence and itself such that the mediation of a maker's making between them can be imagined, so that one of them would be made by that other. Likewise, the agent's effect on existence in the sense of making existence existence is not conceivable, rather his effect is on the essence with respect to existence, in the sense that he makes it qualified by existence, not in the sense that he makes its qualification existent and realized in the external. For the dyer, for example, when he dyes a garment, he does not make the garment a garment, nor the dye dye, but he makes the garment qualified by the dye in the external, even if he does not make its qualification existent and established in the external. Thus essences are not made in themselves, nor are their existences in themselves made, but essences are made in being existent, and this meaning is not to be disputed, and there is no contradiction between negating made-ness from essences in the sense we first mentioned and affirming it for them in the sense we just explained, which is the truth whose falsehood cannot be imagined. So the statement negating made-ness absolutely and affirming it absolutely are both correct if they are interpreted according to what we have pictured.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage is the main argument of the matn. It begins by stating the question: Are essences made or not? It then argues that if an essence is not other than itself, no making is conceivable because there is no distinction between maker and made. But then it introduces a different sense: the agent's effect is on the essence with respect to existence, making it existent. The dyer analogy illustrates this: the dyer does not make the garment a garment, but makes it qualified by dye. So essences are not made in themselves, but are made in being existent. The passage concludes that denying made-ness in one sense and affirming it in another is not contradictory; both statements are correct if properly interpreted.

Key Terms
ماهية (essence)
جعل (making)
وجود (existence)
اتصاف (qualification)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Denying that essences are made means they are uncreated and independent of God.
Correction: The passage clarifies that essences are not made in themselves, but they are made in being existent, meaning their existence comes from God.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, in what sense are essences made?
A) In their own selves
B) In their existence
C) In their qualification
D) Not at all

Key Takeaway
Essences are not made in themselves, but they are made in being existent, and this distinction resolves apparent contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your essence is not made in itself, but only your existence is from Allah, how does this affect your sense of self-reliance and independence?
?
The dyer analogy shows that Allah makes you existent without changing your essence. In what ways do you try to 'make yourself' something you are not, rather than accepting the existence He gives?
?
If your very existence is a qualification given by Allah, what does that demand from you in terms of gratitude and submission right now, in this moment?

Eighth Purpose: Condition for Judging a Quiddity Composed
Al-Jurjani states the condition for judging a quiddity to be composed of parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise condition for judging a quiddity to be composed of parts, including the requirement that the shared differentiator be internal to the quiddity.

Core Idea
A quiddity is composite only when it shares an internal differentiator and differs from another.

Al-Jurjani now establishes the precise condition under which we can say a quiddity (essence) is composed of parts. This is not about mere multiplicity but about genuine composition that yields true unity. The passage clarifies that the shared differentiator must be internal to the quiddity itself, not external.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the entire Arabic passage from the source, including the matn and the commentary. Notice how the author first states the condition and then unpacks each term.
The passage is dense; you may need to read it twice.
2
Identify the condition
Find the key sentence: 'إنما يحكم بكون الماهية مركبة... إذا علم أنها مشاركة لغيرها في ذاتي... ومخالفة'. This states that a quiddity is judged composite when it is known to share an internal differentiator and to differ.
The differentiator (ذاتي) must be internal, not external.
3
Understand 'real composition'
Note the commentary explaining 'مركبة' as a real composition that gives true unity. This distinguishes a mere aggregate from a genuine composite.
Contrast with accidental aggregation.
4
Analyze the role of the differentiator
Reflect on why the differentiator must be internal (غير خارج). The commentary says it includes the entire quiddity, not just a part. This ensures the composition is essential, not accidental.
If the differentiator were external, the composition would be accidental.
5
Consider the examples of genera and differentiae
The passage mentions parts can be genera, differentiae, or others. The key is that both the shared and distinguishing aspects are included. This applies to any essential part.
The 'others' refers to non-predicated parts like matter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الثامن: إنما يحكم بكون الماهية مركبة من أجزاء سواء كانت أجناساً أو فصولاً أو غيرهما، إذا علم أنها مشاركة لغيرها في ذاتي أي أمر غير خارج عنها، ومخالفة. قوله : ( مركبة) أي تركيباً حقيقياً يكون بسببه المركب موصوفاً بالوحدة الحقيقية. قوله : ( أو غيرهما) أي الأجزاء الغير المحمولة. قوله : ( إذا علم إلخ) وفيه إشارة إلى أن تركب الماهية من أمرين متساويين في الصدق وفي التحقيق مجرد احتمال عقلي لا طريق لنا إلى العلم به. قوله : ( أمر) أي سواء كان محمولاً أو غير محمول. قوله : ( غير خارج) لم يفسر الذاتي بالأمر الداخل لأنه لا يحتاج في العلم بتركب الماهية. قوله : ( لكن) يكون قيام بعضها به شرطاً الخ لا يخفى أن مجرد الشرطية لا يكفي في الوحدة الحقيقية فاعتبر اللون المشروط بالضوء على أن توقف الوحدة الحقيقية على ذلك ممنوع؛ لجواز الارتباط بين الأجزاء بوجه آخر. قوله : ( سواء كانت أجناساً أو فصولاً أو غيرها) أي سواء كان بعض تلك الأجزاء أجناساً وبعضها فصولاً أو غيرها بأن يكون ما به الاشتراك فصلاً بعيداً، وما به الامتياز فصلاً قريباً مثلاً، فإن المقصود هاهنا لزوم دخول ما به الاشتراك وما به الامتياز ليس إلا. وحمل الغير على الأجزاء الخارجية، أو التعين يأباه السياق. قوله : ( أي أمر غير خارج) إنما فسر الذاتي بهذا ليشمل تمام الماهية، إذ لو أريد به الجزء لقال: داخل.
The eighth purpose: One only judges a quiddity to be composed of parts, whether they be genera or differentiae or others, when it is known that it shares with another in a differentiator (dhati) — i.e., something not external to it — and differs (from it). His statement: 'composed' i.e., a real composition by which the composite is described as having true unity. His statement: 'or others' i.e., the parts that are not predicated. His statement: 'when it is known etc.' In it there is an indication that the composition of a quiddity from two things that are equal in truth and in reality is a mere rational possibility with no way for us to know it. His statement: 'thing' i.e., whether predicated or not. His statement: 'not external' — he did not interpret 'dhati' as the internal thing because there is no need in knowing the composition of the quiddity. His statement: 'but' — the subsistence of some of them in it is a condition etc. It is not hidden that mere conditionality is insufficient for true unity, so consider color conditioned by light, for the dependence of true unity on that is denied, because connection between parts can be through another mode. His statement: 'whether they be genera or differentiae or others' i.e., whether some of those parts are genera and some differentiae or others, such that what is shared is a distant differentia and what distinguishes is a near differentia, for example, for the purpose here is nothing but the necessity of including what is shared and what distinguishes. And interpreting 'others' as external parts or determination is precluded by the context. His statement: 'i.e., something not external' — he only interpreted 'dhati' in this way to include the entire quiddity, for if he intended the part, he would have said 'internal'.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage is a statement of the eighth purpose: the condition for judging a quiddity to be composed. Al-Jurjani says that one only judges a quiddity to be composed of parts (whether genera, differentiae, or other) if it is known that it shares with another in a differentiator (dhati) that is not external to it, and that it differs from that other. The commentary explains that 'composed' means real composition leading to true unity. 'Others' refers to non-predicated parts. The commentary also notes that if a quiddity could be composed of two equal things, that is only a rational possibility, not knowable. The interpretation of 'dhati' as 'not external' is to include the entire quiddity, not just a part. The commentary further argues that mere conditionality (like subsistence as a condition) is insufficient for true unity; there must be a real connection, possibly through another mode. The passage also clarifies that the parts can be various combinations of genera and differentiae, and the essential point is the inclusion of both the shared and distinguishing aspects.

Key Terms
الماهية
مركبة
أجزاء
ذاتي
مشاركة
مخالفة
الوحدة الحقيقية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that any shared and distinguishing features suffice for composition, even if the differentiator is external to the quiddity.
Correction: Al-Jurjani emphasizes that the differentiator must be internal (غير خارج) to the quiddity; otherwise, the composition is not essential. The differentiator must be part of the quiddity's essence.

Checkpoint
According to the eighth purpose, when is a quiddity judged to be composed?
A) When it has parts that are independent.
B) When it is known to share a differentiator and differ from another.
C) When it is simple.
D) When it has genera only.

Key Takeaway
A quiddity is judged composite only when it shares an internal differentiator and differs from another, ensuring essential composition leading to true unity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you assess whether something in your life is truly unified—like your intentions, character, or a relationship—do you focus on internal differentiators (what is essential) or merely external commonalities? How does al-Jurjani's condition challenge you to be more honest about the foundations of that unity?
?
Al-Jurjani warns that composition from two equal things is a mere rational possibility that we cannot know. Consider a belief or conviction you hold that seems perfectly logically coherent—how do you know it is genuinely composed in reality and not just a rational possibility? What would it take for you to seek certainty beyond reason?
